Principles of Preference When Hanafis Differ (Principles 5-13)
List of rules for preferring one Hanafi opinion over another

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the nine principles for preferring one Hanafi opinion over another and identify which opinion is mufta bih in specific domains.

Core Idea
When Hanafis differ, the mufti follows a hierarchy: Imam in ibadat, Abu Yusuf in judiciary, Muhammad in inheritance, Zufar in 17 issues.

This lesson presents nine key principles that guide a mufti when faced with conflicting opinions within the Hanafi school. These rules, derived from the works of later Hanafi masters, establish a clear hierarchy for determining the relied-upon (mufta bih) position based on the nature of the disagreement and the specific fiqh domain. Understanding these principles is essential for issuing accurate fatwas.

Guided Path
1
Identify the type of disagreement
Determine whether the conflict is between Imam Abu Hanifa and the Sahibayn (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad), or among later students and mashayikh, and whether it is based on changing circumstances.
Principles 5-8 address these distinctions.
2
Apply domain-specific rules
For acts of worship (ibadat), prefer Abu Hanifa's view; for judiciary (qada), prefer Abu Yusuf's view; for inheritance (mirath), prefer Muhammad's view; for seventeen specific issues, prefer Zufar's view.
Principles 10-13 give clear assignments.
3
Consider the source of the opinion
When no domain rule applies, revert to the rank of the scholar: the Sahibayn's views generally outweigh later students, and mashayikh's views are considered when no earlier opinion is strong.
Principles 8-9 cover this hierarchy.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
پانچواں اصول: جب کسی مسئلہ میں احناف کا اختلاف ہو۔ چھٹا اصول: جب امام اعظم اور صاحبین کا اختلاف عصر و زمان پر مبنی ہو۔ ساتواں اصول: جب امام اعظم اور صاحبین کا اختلاف عصر و زمان پر مبنی نہ ہو۔ آٹھواں اصول: امام اعظم کے شاگردوں کے اقوال پر فتوی دینا۔ نواں اصول: مشائخ کے اقوال پر فتوی دینا۔ دسواں اصول: عبادات میں امام اعظم کا قول مفتی بہ ہے۔ گیارہواں اصول: قضاء کے باب میں امام ابو یوسف کا قول مفتی بہ ہے۔ بارہواں اصول: وراثت میں امام محمد کا قول مفتی بہ ہے۔ تیرہواں اصول: سترہ مسائل میں امام زفر کا قول مفتی بہ ہے۔
Fifth Principle: When there is disagreement among Hanafis regarding an issue. Sixth Principle: When the disagreement between Imam A'zam and the Sahibayn is based on time and circumstance. Seventh Principle: When the disagreement between Imam A'zam and the Sahibayn is not based on time and circumstance. Eighth Principle: Giving fatwa based on the statements of Imam A'zam's students. Ninth Principle: Giving fatwa based on the statements of the mashayikh. Tenth Principle: In acts of worship, Imam A'zam's statement is the mufta bih. Eleventh Principle: In the chapter of judiciary, Imam Abu Yusuf's statement is the mufta bih. Twelfth Principle: In inheritance, Imam Muhammad's statement is the mufta bih. Thirteenth Principle: In seventeen issues, Imam Zufar's statement is the mufta bih.
Source pages p. 5

Commentary
This passage enumerates nine principles (5th to 13th) that guide the mufti when choosing among conflicting Hanafi opinions. The principles address disagreements between Imam Abu Hanifa and his two companions (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad), when to follow the Imam vs the students, and specific areas like worship, judiciary, inheritance, and the unique position of Imam Zufar in seventeen issues. The mufti must apply these rules to determine the correct fatwa in cases of ikhtilaf.

Key Terms
امام اعظم (Imam A'zam): Imam Abu Hanifa
صاحبین (Sahibayn): Abu Yusuf and Muhammad al-Shaybani, the two main disciples of Abu Hanifa
مفتی بہ (mufta bih): The relied-upon opinion for issuing fatwa

Key Terms from Source Passage

Key Takeaway

Six Principles of Book Preference in Hanafi Fatwa
Hierarchical principles for preferring rulings based on textual authority

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the six principles of preference based on books in the Hanafi madhhab and apply them to determine the authoritative ruling in a fatwa scenario.

Core Idea
Zahir al-Riwayah is primary; mutun precede commentaries; fiqh books precede usul books.

When a mufti faces conflicting rulings, he must know which source to prefer. This lesson presents six hierarchical principles from the classical Hanafi methodology (Rasm al-Mufti) that guide the mufti in choosing the most authoritative ruling. Mastery of these principles ensures that fatwas are based on the strongest transmitted evidence.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Zahir al-Riwayah ruling
The mufti must first check if the ruling is in one of the six core books of Imam Muhammad (Zahir al-Riwayah). If found, that ruling is obligatory to follow per principle 1.
This is the foundational step.
2
If multiple Zahir al-Riwayah rulings, determine the latest composition
If there are multiple rulings within Zahir al-Riwayah, the later authored book takes precedence (principle 2). For example, Al-Jami' al-Saghir is later than Al-Mabsut.
Only applies within Zahir al-Riwayah.
3
If no Zahir al-Riwayah, check non-Zahir al-Riwayah
If the ruling is not in Zahir al-Riwayah, secondary sources (nawadir, fatawa collections) may be used, but they are subordinate (principle 3).
Non-Zahir al-Riwayah is not obligatory.
4
Compare sources: mutun vs shuruh vs fatawa
Among secondary sources, original texts (mutun) take precedence over commentaries (shuruh), and commentaries over fatwa collections (principles 4 and 5).
E.g., Al-Quduri's Mukhtasar over its sharh.
5
If conflict remains, prefer fiqh books over usul books
When a fiqh work and a usul work conflict, the fiqh work is preferred (principle 6).
Usul works give theoretical principles, not specific rulings.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
کتابوں کی بنیاد پر ترجیح کے ۶ اصول۔ پہلا اصول: ظاہر الروایہ پر فتوی دینا واجب ہے۔ دوسرا اصول: ظاہر الروایہ میں آخری تصنیف کا اعتبار ہوگا۔ تیسرا اصول: غیر ظاہر الروایہ پر فتوی دینا۔ چوتھا اصول: متون کے مسائل شروح وفتاوی پر مقدم ہوں گے۔ پانچواں اصول: شروح کے مسائل فتاوی پر مقدم ہوں گے۔ چھٹا اصول: فقہی کتابیں اصولی کتابوں پر مقدم ہوں گی۔
Six principles of preference based on books. First Principle: It is obligatory to give fatwa according to Zahir al-Riwayah. Second Principle: In Zahir al-Riwayah, the later composition will be considered. Third Principle: Giving fatwa according to non-Zahir al-Riwayah. Fourth Principle: The issues of the mutun take precedence over sharh and fatawa. Fifth Principle: The issues of the sharh take precedence over fatawa. Sixth Principle: Fiqh books take precedence over usul books.
Source pages p. 5

Commentary
This passage lists six hierarchical principles for preferring one ruling over another based on the type of book. The mufti must first rely on rulings from Zahir al-Riwayah (the six core books of Imam Muhammad). Within Zahir al-Riwayah, the later authored book takes precedence. Non-Zahir al-Riwayah rulings can be used only secondary. Furthermore, the original texts (mutun) are preferred over commentaries (shuruh), and commentaries over fatwa collections. Finally, books of substantive fiqh outweigh books of usul al-fiqh in determining fatwa.

Key Terms
ظاہر الروایہ (Zahir al-Riwayah): The six core books of Imam Muhammad al-Shaybani containing well-known transmissions from Abu Hanifa
متن (matn): The main text of a fiqh work (e.g., Al-Mukhtasar by Al-Quduri)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
If a ruling is found in both Zahir al-Riwayah and a later fatwa collection, which should the mufti follow?
A) The fatwa collection
B) Zahir al-Riwayah
C) The later one
D) Both equally

Key Takeaway
The mufti must prioritize Zahir al-Riwayah, then later compositions within it, then non-Zahir al-Riwayah, preferring mutun over shuruh over fatawa, and fiqh over usul.

Five Principles of Tarjih (Preference) Based on Evidence
Rules for preferring a ruling based on strength of evidence

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the five principles of tarjih and apply each to a hypothetical fatwa scenario.

Core Idea
When proofs conflict, prefer well-known hadith, istihsan over qiyas, and textual consistency.

A mufti often encounters conflicting evidence. The Hanafi tradition provides clear principles for preferring one ruling over another. These five rules, derived from the works of the early masters, ensure consistency and methodological rigour. Memorise them as your tarjih toolkit.

Guided Path
1
State the First Principle
If one opinion is based on a well-known hadith (mashhurah), give it precedence over opinions based on weaker reports or analogy.
Well-known here means widely transmitted among the Companions or early scholars.
2
State the Second Principle
If a mujtahid's statement apparently contradicts a hadith, investigate the reason. The hadith may be abrogated, qualified, or misunderstood. Do not automatically reject the mujtahid's view.
This principle prevents hasty rejection of established school positions.
3
State the Third Principle
Istihsan (juristic preference) takes precedence over qiyas (analogy). When qiyas leads to a harsh or inequitable result, istihsan can override it based on a stronger evidence.
This is a distinctive Hanafi method; it is not arbitrary but disciplined.
4
State the Fourth Principle
Prefer explicit textual coherence (sarih al-iltizam) over correction (tashih). That is, give priority to a reading that is consistent with the clear wording rather than a forced interpretation.
The exact wording is debated; some read 'sarih al-tazam' or 'sarih al-tashih'.
5
State the Fifth Principle
Follow the style and methodology of the original authors (e.g., Quduri, Marghinani) when determining tarjih. Their implicit preferences are authoritative.
This ensures continuity within the school.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
دلیل کی بنیاد پر ترجیح کے ۵ اصول۔ پہلا اصول: جب ایک قول کی بنیاد مشہور حدیث ہو۔ دوسرا اصول: مجتہد کا جو قول بظاہر حدیث کے خلاف ہو۔ تیسرا اصول: استحسان کو قیاس پر ترجیح حاصل ہوگی۔ چوتھا اصول: ترجیح صریح التزامی تصیح پر مقدم ہوگی۔ پانچواں اصول: مصنفین کے اسلوب کی پیروی کرنا لازم ہے۔
Five principles of preference based on evidence. First Principle: When a statement is based on a well-known hadith. Second Principle: What a mujtahid's statement that apparently contradicts a hadith. Third Principle: Istihsan takes precedence over qiyas. Fourth Principle: Preference of explicit coherence over correction. Fifth Principle: It is necessary to follow the style of the authors.
Source pages p. 5

Commentary
This passage outlines five principles for preferring one scholarly opinion over another based on the strength of its evidence. The first principle gives priority to opinions supported by well-known hadith. The second principle addresses statements of a mujtahid that appear to contradict a hadith. The third principle establishes that istihsan (juristic preference) outweighs qiyas (analogy). The fourth principle prefers textual consistency over correction. The fifth principle requires the mufti to follow the methodology and style of the original authors when determining the tarjih.

Key Terms
تَّرْجِيح (tarjih): Preferring one legal opinion over another due to stronger evidence.
مشہور حدیث (hadith mashhurah): A hadith transmitted by at least two Companions or widely known among scholars.

Key Terms from Source Passage

D) Ijma

Key Takeaway
Memorise these five principles: they are your compass for navigating conflicting evidences in fatwa.

Five Styles for Presenting the Ruling (Uslub al-Tarjih)
Enumeration of five uslub al-tarjih

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify and apply the five styles for presenting a preponderant opinion in Hanafi fatwa works.

Core Idea
The mufti has five distinct styles for presenting the rajih opinion, each serving a different pedagogical or argumentative purpose.

In Hanafi fatwa literature, muftis often encounter multiple opinions on a single issue. The technique for presenting the preferred opinion is known as uslub al-tarjih. This passage enumerates five distinct styles that a mufti may employ, from simply giving precedence to the rajih statement, to refuting weaker opinions, or handling cases with three opinions. Understanding these styles helps the student both read fatwa works critically and present rulings effectively.

Guided Path
1
Memorize the Five Styles
Learn the five styles by heart: 1) give precedence to the rajih statement, 2) delay the evidence of the rajih statement, 3) mention only the evidence of the rajih statement, 4) refute the non-preponderant statement, 5) handle cases with three statements.
These are the exact formulations from the passage.
2
Understand the Purpose of Each Style
For each style, consider what the mufti aims to achieve. Style 1 is straightforward. Style 2 might be used when the evidence is complex and needs to be explained later. Style 3 omits weaker opinions to avoid confusion. Style 4 actively refutes errors. Style 5 acknowledges three opinions and then selects the rajih.
The purpose is not explicit in the passage but can be inferred.
3
Distinguish Between Similar Styles
Compare Style 1 (precedence) vs. Style 2 (delayed evidence) vs. Style 3 (only evidence). In Style 1, the rajih statement is presented first; in Style 2, it may be presented later; in Style 3, only the evidence is given. Also, Style 4 uniquely includes refutation.
Note the differences in what is included (statement, evidence, refutation) and sequence.
4
Apply to a Hypothetical Fatwa Scenario
Imagine a fatwa issue with two opinions: one strong (rajih) and one weak. Choose which style to use depending on the audience. For a layperson, Style 3 might be best. For a scholarly work, Style 4 might be appropriate. For a pedagogical setting, Style 2 could be useful.
There is no single correct answer; the choice depends on context.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
پہلا اسلوب: راجح قول کو مقدم کرنا۔ دوسرا اسلوب: راجح قول کی دلیل کو مؤخر کرنا۔ تیسرا اسلوب: صرف راجح قول کی دلیل ذکر کرنا۔ چوتھا اسلوب: غیر راجح قول کی تردید کرنا۔ پانچواں اسلوب: جب کسی مسئلہ میں تین اقوال ہوں۔
First Style: Giving precedence to the preponderant statement. Second Style: Delaying the evidence of the preponderant statement. Third Style: Mentioning only the evidence of the preponderant statement. Fourth Style: Refuting the non-preponderant statement. Fifth Style: When there are three statements on an issue.
Source pages p. 6

Commentary
This passage presents five stylistic approaches that a mufti or author may use when dealing with multiple opinions. The first and most straightforward is to simply give priority to the preponderant (rajih) opinion. The second style involves mentioning the evidence for the rajih opinion later, perhaps for pedagogical reasons. The third style means omitting evidence of weaker opinions entirely. The fourth style explicitly refutes the non-preponderant opinion. The fifth style addresses cases where there are three opinions, implying a specific method for handling such multiplicity.

Key Terms
اسلوب (uslub): Style or method of presenting the tarjih
راجح (rajih): The preponderant or preferred opinion

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) First style
B) Second style
C) Third style
D) Fourth style

Key Takeaway
The mufti's choice of presentation style is a deliberate technique to effectively communicate the rajih opinion.

Principles of Preference Based on Words of Tarjih
This passage is a list of ten principles for interpreting words of tarjih in Hanafi fatwa methodology.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify and apply the ten Hanafi principles of preference based on words of tarjih in fatwa literature.

Core Idea
Words of tarjih like 'sahih', 'asahh', 'fatwa' are not interchangeable; each carries a distinct weight in determining the mufta bih.

In Hanafi fiqh works, scholars use specific technical terms to indicate which opinion is preferred. The mufti must interpret these terms correctly. This lesson outlines the ten principles that govern the understanding of these words of tarjih.

Guided Path
1
Memorize the ten principles
Internalize the list of ten principles as axioms for understanding tarjih terminology.
Each principle is a separate rule; learn them in order.
2
Classify each principle by the word used
Identify which term (sahih, asahh, fatwa, etc.) each principle revolves around.
This helps associate the principle with the specific word of tarjih.
3
Apply each principle to a concrete example
Imagine a scenario where conflicting opinions are present and the text uses one of these terms. Determine which opinion is preferred based on the principle.
Practice with hypothetical cases.
4
Recognize the hierarchy
Note that 'asahh' indicates a higher degree than 'sahih', and 'fatwa' indicates the relied-upon opinion. When multiple terms appear, prioritize according to these principles.
Remember principle six: when both 'sahih' and 'asahh' are used, 'asahh' prevails.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الفاظ ترجیح کی بنیاد پر ترجیح کے ۱۰ اصول۔ پہلا اصول: جب صحیح اجتماعی الفاظ سے ہو۔ دوسرا اصول: جب کسی قول کی تصحیح لفظ فتوی سے کی جائے۔ تیسرا اصول: جب دونوں اقوال کی ترجیح لفظ فتوی سے کی جائے۔ چوتھا اصول: جب صحیح کرنے والے علماء کا درجہ ایک ہو۔ پانچواں اصول: جب تصحیح کرنے والے علماء کے درجہ میں فرق ہو۔ چھٹا اصول: جب صحیح کے لیے صحیح اور اصح کا لفظ مستعمل ہو۔ ساتواں اصول: ایک ہی امام کا ایک قول کو صحیح اور دوسرے کو اصح کہنا۔ آٹھواں اصول: مصنف کا کسی ایک فقیہ کی تصحیح کو اصح قرار دینا۔ نواں اصول: جب تصحیح کے لیے اسم تفضیل کا صیغہ مستعمل ہو۔ دسواں اصول: جب صرف ایک قول کی تصحیح کی جائے۔
Ten principles of preference based on words of tarjih. First Principle: When 'sahih' is used with collective words. Second Principle: When a statement is corrected with the word 'fatwa'. Third Principle: When both statements are given preference with the word 'fatwa'. Fourth Principle: When the rank of the scholars making the correction is the same. Fifth Principle: When the ranks of the scholars making the correction differ. Sixth Principle: When the words 'sahih' and 'asahh' are used for 'sahih'. Seventh Principle: When the same imam calls one statement 'sahih' and another 'asahh'. Eighth Principle: When the author considers the correction of one particular faqih as asahh. Ninth Principle: When the superlative form is used for correction. Tenth Principle: When only one statement is corrected.
Source pages p. 6

Commentary
This passage lists ten principles for interpreting the technical terms used in fiqh works to indicate preference. The mufti must understand what 'sahih' (correct), 'asahh' (more correct), 'fatwa' (the relied opinion) etc. convey. For instance, if a scholar uses the word 'sahih' in a collective context, that carries weight. When the word 'fatwa' is used to correct a statement, that statement is preferred. If two conflicting corrections come from scholars of equal rank, a different method applies than if ranks differ. The use of comparative (asahh) indicates a higher level of preference. These principles help the mufti navigate the often subtle nuances in the language of tarjih.

Key Terms
صحیح (sahih): Correct or sound; a technical term of tarjih indicating the preferred opinion
اصح (asahh): More correct; a higher degree of preference than 'sahih'
فتوی (fatwa): The relied-upon opinion for issuing fatwa

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) The one called 'sahih'
B) The one called 'asahh'
C) Both equally
D) Neither

Key Takeaway
The ten principles of tarjih based on words of tarjih provide the mufti with a clear hierarchy for selecting the most authoritative opinion.

The First Five Principles of Tatbiq (Reconciliation) for Conflicting Tarjih
List of reconciliation principles

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the first five principles of tatbiq and apply them to prioritize conflicting Hanafi tarjihs.

Core Idea
When mashayikh differ, defined terms form a hierarchy: 'asahh' beats 'sahih', 'fatwa' beats 'sahih', and the statements of Imam A'zam, Zahir al-Riwayah, and mutun each outrank later commentaries.

In the Hanafi fatwa tradition, later scholars (mashayikh) sometimes give conflicting preferments. Rather than confusion, distinct terms like 'sahih', 'asahh', 'fatwa', etc., indicate a built-in hierarchy. This lesson presents the first five of fourteen principles that muftis use to resolve such conflicts, ensuring that fatwas are based on the most authoritative layer of the school.

Guided Path
1
Memorize the first principle
'Asahh' prevails over 'sahih'. Recognize that 'asahh' indicates a higher degree of correctness.
2
Memorize the second principle
A ruling marked with 'fatwa' (i.e., the relied-upon position) overrides one merely called 'sahih'.
3
Memorize the third principle
Imam Abu Hanifa's own statement takes precedence over later scholars' views.
4
Memorize the fourth principle
Statements from Zahir al-Riwayah (the core Hanafi corpus) outweigh those from nawadir (rare transmissions).
5
Memorize the fifth principle
Rulings in the mutun (concise authoritative texts) prevail over explanations in sharh and fatwa collections.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
مشائخ کی ترجیحات میں تطبیق کے ۱۴ اصول۔ پہلا اصول: اصح صحیح کے مقابلہ میں راجح ہوگا۔ دوسرا اصول: فتوی والا قول صحیح کے مقابلہ میں رائج ہوگا۔ تیسرا اصول: امام اعظم کا قول راجح ہوگا۔ چوتھا اصول: ظاہر الروایہ کا قول راجح ہوگا۔ پانچواں اصول: متون کا قول شروح و فتاوی پر رائج ہوگا۔
Fourteen principles for reconciling the preferments of the mashayikh. First Principle: The 'asahh' will prevail over 'sahih'. Second Principle: The statement with 'fatwa' will be current over 'sahih'. Third Principle: Imam A'zam's statement will be preponderant. Fourth Principle: The statement of Zahir al-Riwayah will be preponderant. Fifth Principle: The statement of the mutun will prevail over sharh and fatawa.
Source pages p. 6

Commentary
This passage lists the first five of fourteen principles for resolving conflicts when later scholars (mashayikh) have made different tarjihs. The first principle gives priority to 'asahh' over 'sahih'. The second principle states that a ruling marked with 'fatwa' outweighs one simply called 'sahih'. The third principle returns to the authority of Imam Abu Hanifa himself. The fourth principle prioritizes rulings from Zahir al-Riwayah. The fifth principle continues the earlier hierarchy of textual sources: mutun over sharh and fatawa. These principles help the mufti when even the later authorities disagree on which ruling is correct.

Key Terms
مشائخ (mashayikh): Later authoritative Hanafi scholars after the early imams
تطبیق (tatbiq): Reconciliation of conflicting preferences

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

B) The 'asahh'
C) Neither
D) Both are equal

Key Takeaway
The first five principles of tatbiq establish a clear hierarchy: asahh > saheeh, fatwa > saheeh, Imam A'zam > others, Zahir al-Riwayah > nawadir, mutun > shuruh/fatawa.

Five Methods for Presenting the Rajih Qawl
Lists five methods for presenting the preferred opinion.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify and apply the five methods (asalib) for presenting the preferred opinion (rajih qawl) in a Hanafi fatwa.

Core Idea
Five stylistic methods for a mufti to effectively present the preferred opinion.

A mufti often faces multiple valid opinions within the madhhab. To convey the correct ruling clearly, he must choose how to present the preferred (rajih) opinion. This passage from 'Uqud Rasm al-Mufti' lists five stylistic methods (asalib) that a mufti may employ depending on the context and the questioner's needs.

Guided Path
1
Recognize multiple opinions
Identify that there are multiple valid opinions in the madhhab on the issue at hand. Understand which one is rajih (preferred) based on later scholars' tarjih.
The rajih qawl is the one relied upon for fatwa.
2
Choose a method from the five
Based on the questioner's level and the nature of the proofs, select one of the five methods: present rajih first, delay its proof, mention only its proof, refute the weak opinion, or handle three opinions.
Each method has a specific purpose.
3
Apply the method in the fatwa text
Write the fatwa answer using the chosen structure. For example, if using the first method, state the rajih ruling immediately.
Maintain clarity and respect for all opinions.
4
Ensure the fatwa is mufta bihi
Confirm that the ruling presented is indeed the mufta bihi (operative) position in the Hanafi school. The method should not alter the ruling.
The method is a presentation technique, not a tarjih criterion.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
پہلا اسلوب : راجح قول کو مقدم کرنا . دوسرا اسلوب : راجح قول کی دلیل کو مؤخر کرنا . تیسرا اسلوب : صرف راجح قول کی دلیل ذکر کرنا. چوتھا اسلوب : غیر راجح قول کی تردید کرنا۔ پانچواں اسلوب : جب کسی مسئلہ میں تین اقوال ہوں ۔
First method: To present the preferred opinion first. Second method: To delay the proof of the preferred opinion. Third method: To mention only the proof of the preferred opinion. Fourth method: To refute the non-preferred opinion. Fifth method: When there are three opinions in a matter.
Source pages p. 6

Commentary
This passage from Naseem Ul Mufti's 'Sharh Uqud Rasm al-Mufti' outlines five stylistic methods (asalib) for a mufti to articulate the preferred (rajih) position in a fatwa. The first method is straightforward: lead with the rajih qawl. The second method involves not immediately giving the proof for the rajih, perhaps to encourage reflection. The third method gives only the proof for the rajih. The fourth method explicitly refutes the weaker view. The fifth method applies when three opinions exist; the text is cut off but likely indicates a specific handling. These methods help the mufti convey the correct ruling effectively.

Key Terms
اسلوب (uslub): A method or style of presenting the preferred opinion in a fatwa.
راجح قول (rajih qawl): The preferred or strongest opinion among multiple valid opinions in the madhhab.

Key Takeaway

Principles of Reconciling Preferences of the Mashayikh
Lists fourteen principles for reconciling conflicting preferences among leading Hanafi scholars.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the fourteen principles of tarjih and apply them to resolve conflicting opinions within the Hanafi madhhab.

Core Idea
Fourteen principles guide the Hanafi mufti when the mashayikh differ; not all carry equal weight.

The Hanafi madhhab has a rich tradition of scholarly discussion. When leading scholars (mashayikh) differ on a ruling, the mufti must know which opinion to prefer. This lesson presents fourteen principles (usul) for reconciling such differences. Memorizing these principles equips you to navigate apparent conflicts in the tradition.

Guided Path
1
MEMORIZE the list
Learn the fourteen principles in order. Each is a distinct rule of preference. Use mnemonic devices if helpful.
The principles are not ordered by importance; they are to be applied contextually.
2
CATEGORIZE each principle
Group principles by type: textual authority (1-6), methodological preference (7), practical objectives (8-14). Understanding the categories helps you recall them.
For example, principles 3-5 relate to the source hierarchy within the madhhab.
3
APPLY to a hypothetical conflict
Given two conflicting opinions, identify which principles apply. Determine which principle should take precedence based on the specific context (e.g., a waqf case vs. a criminal case).
Sometimes multiple principles point to the same opinion; sometimes they conflict, requiring careful judgement.

Third principle: 14 principles of reconciling the preferences of the mashayikh. First principle: Asahh will prevail over sahih. Second principle: The opinion with 'fatwa' will be followed over sahih. Third principle: The opinion of Imam A'zam (Abu Hanifa) will be preferred. Fourth principle: The opinion of Zahir al-Riwayah will be preferred. Fifth principle: The opinion of the matn (text) will be followed over commentaries and fatwas. Sixth principle: The opinion of the senior fuqaha will be preferred. Seventh principle: Istihsan prevails over qiyas. Eighth principle: The opinion that benefits the waqf (endowment) will be preferred. Ninth principle: The easier opinion will be preferred. Tenth principle: The opinion based on strong evidence will be preferred. Eleventh principle: The opinion that benefits the poor will be preferred. Twelfth principle: The opinion that averts hudud (fixed penalties) will be preferred. Thirteenth principle: The opinion that declares something haram will be preferred. Fourteenth principle: The opinion based on maslahah (public interest) will be preferred.
Source pages p. 6

Commentary
This passage enumerates fourteen principles (usul) for preferring one over another when the mashayikh (leading scholars of the madhhab) differ. These principles cover various aspects: textual authority (asahh over sahih, fatwa-labeled over sahih), hierarchy of sources (Imam A'zam, Zahir al-Riwayah, matn over commentaries), methodological preferences (istihsan over qiyas), and practical objectives (benefit to waqf or poor, ease, aversion of hudud, prohibition, and maslahah). The mufti uses these to resolve apparent conflicts in the tradition.

Key Terms
مشائخ (mashayikh): Senior scholars of the Hanafi madhhab, typically those from the later generations (mutakhkhirin).
ظاہر الروایہ (Zahir al-Riwayah): The well-known and transmitted positions from Imam Muhammad's six books.
اصح (asahh): The more sound of two opinions within the madhhab.
صحیح (sahih): A sound opinion, but not necessarily the strongest.
استحسان (istihsan): Juristic preference; departing from qiyas for a stronger reason (e.g., ease, custom).

Key Terms from Source Passage

Key Takeaway
When mashayikh differ, the mufti weighs textual strength, authority, and practical benefit to choose the preferred opinion.

Principles 6-14 for Tarjih Among Hanafi Opinions
Listing principles for preferring one opinion over another

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the fourteen principles for tarjih among opinions and apply them to select the preponderant opinion in a given scenario.

Core Idea
A Hanafi mufti weighs conflicting opinions using fourteen principles — from seniority to public interest.

When conflicting opinions exist within the Hanafi madhhab, the mufti must determine which is preponderant (rajih). This lesson covers principles 6 through 14 from the classical work 'Nasim al-Mufti'. These rules guide the mufti to prefer opinions based on the authority of the jurist, the strength of evidence, practical benefit, ease, and public interest. Mastering these principles is essential for sound fatwa practice.

Guided Path
1
Identify the principles
List the nine principles from the text: 6th (senior jurists), 7th (istihsan over qiyas), 8th (benefit for waqf), 9th (ease), 10th (strong evidence), 11th (benefit for poor), 12th (nullifying hudud), 13th (prohibition), 14th (public interest).
These are not exhaustive but are key tools for tarjih.
2
Categorize by basis
Group principles by their underlying rationale: authority (6th), evidence (7th, 10th), benefit (8th, 11th, 14th), ease (9th), and legal effect (12th, 13th).
Understanding the basis helps apply them correctly.
3
Recognize potential conflicts
Note that principles may point to different outcomes. For example, ease (9th) may conflict with prohibition (13th). The mufti must weigh them, often giving precedence to stronger evidence or established authority.
The text does not specify a fixed hierarchy; later scholars may provide ordering.
4
Apply to a scenario
Given two opinions, identify which principles apply. If one opinion is based on istihsan and the other on qiyas, the 7th principle favors istihsan. If both opinions are equally evidenced, the 9th principle (ease) may tip the scale.
Practice with hypothetical cases to internalize the principles.

Principles of Reliable and Unreliable Books for Fatwa
Defining principles for identifying reliable books and causes of unreliability

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the ten causes of book unreliability and apply the fourth and fifth principles of fatwa-giving.

Core Idea
A mufti must give fatwa only from reliable books, avoiding ten specific causes of unreliability.

This passage introduces two key principles for muftis: the obligation to rely on reliable books and the prohibition of using unreliable ones. It then enumerates ten causes that render a book unreliable, covering authorial, textual, and transmission issues. Understanding these causes is essential for scholarly integrity in fatwa practice.

Guided Path
1
Internalize the two principles
Learn the fourth and fifth principles: give fatwa from reliable books and avoid giving fatwa from unreliable books.
These are foundational for a mufti's source selection.
2
Memorize the ten causes
List the ten causes and group them: author-related (unknown author, weak author, doubt in attribution), content-related (weak opinions, extreme brevity, non-fiqh discipline), and quality-related (declared weak by a verifying jurist, rarity, printing errors, unclear handwriting).
Grouping aids recall.
3
Understand the rationale
For each cause, explain why it makes a book unreliable, e.g., an unknown author cannot be trusted; weak opinions may lead to incorrect fatwas.
Reasoning strengthens application.
4
Apply to a scenario
Given a book with an unknown author and weak opinions, decide if it is reliable and which principles apply.
Practice with real cases.

These causes cover issues with the author (unknown, declared weak), the content (weak opinions, extreme brevity), transmission (doubt in attribution), rarity, and textual quality (printing errors, unclear script). The list is a practical guide for muftis to select authoritative sources.

ضعیف (da'eef): Weak, not reliable for fatwa.

Principles of Rasm al-Mufti: Shuyukh and Sahibayn
Stating principles regarding giving fatwa from shuyukh and inclusion of Sahibayn

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the eighth and ninth principles of fatwa, identify the shuyukh and the Sahibayn, and apply the hierarchy of their authority in issuing a fatwa.

Core Idea
The students of Abu Hanifa, especially the Sahibayn, are authoritative sources for fatwa; their opinions are integral to the Hanafi school.

This lesson presents two foundational principles from the Rasm al-Mufti tradition. The eighth principle directs the mufti to rely on the statements of the direct students of Imam Abu Hanifa (the shuyukh). The ninth principle questions whether the opinions of the two famous companions, Abu Yusuf and Muhammad al-Shaybani (the Sahibayn), are part of the Hanafi madhhab. Understanding these principles is crucial for a mufti to correctly navigate the Hanafi hierarchy of evidence and ensure their fatwa aligns with the school's authoritative positions.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two principles
Read the passage and separate the eighth and ninth principles. The eighth principle is about the shuyukh; the ninth is about the Sahibayn.
The passage presents them as distinct points.
2
Understand the eighth principle
Who are the 'students' (shuyukh) of Imam A'zam? They include Abu Yusuf, Muhammad al-Shaybani, Zufar, and Hasan ibn Ziyad. The principle says fatwa should be based on their statements.
These are the immediate students, not later scholars.
3
Understand the ninth principle
The ninth principle poses a question: Are the sayings of the Sahibayn included in Hanafi fiqh? The passage does not answer directly, but from our commentary we learn they are indeed included and authoritative.
This clarifies that the Sahibayn are part of the shuyukh, so the eighth principle already covers them.
4
Apply the hierarchy
In practice, a mufti should first check the opinions of the Sahibayn among the shuyukh. If they have a clear statement, it is preferred over many later opinions, especially in mu'amalat.
This reflects the principle of preferring the views of the earlier authorities.

آٹھواں اصول: امام اعظم کے شاگردوں کے اقوال پر فتوی دینا۔
نواں اصول: فقہ حنفی میں صاحبین کے اقوال شامل ہیں یا نہیں؟
Eighth principle: Giving fatwa based on the sayings of the students of Imam A'zam (Abu Hanifa).
Ninth principle: Are the sayings of the two Sahibayn (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad) included in Hanafi fiqh?

Commentary
This passage introduces two more principles. The eighth directs the mufti to give fatwa according to the statements of the direct students of Imam Abu Hanifa, such as Abu Yusuf and Muhammad al-Shaybani, as well as others like Zufar and Hasan ibn Ziyad. The ninth principle raises the question whether the opinions of Sahibayn are part of the Hanafi madhhab. The text does not provide the answer here, but in Hanafi usul, their views are considered authoritative and often preferred over later opinions, though the madhhab itself is attributed to Imam Abu Hanifa.

Key Terms
صاحبین (Saahibayn): The two companions: Imam Abu Yusuf and Imam Muhammad al-Shaybani.
امام اعظم (Imaam A'zam): The Greatest Imam, referring to Abu Hanifa.

Key Takeaway

The Mufti's Obligation: Following the Rajih or Zahir al-Riwayah
States the obligation of following the preponderant opinion or that recorded in Zahir al-Riwayah.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the Hanafi rule that a mufti must follow the rajih opinion or that recorded in Zahir al-Riwayah, and identify the role of ahl al-tarjih.

Core Idea
A mufti must follow the preponderant opinion; tarjih belongs only to its qualified people.

This passage is a core rule from the science of Rasm al-Mufti, which governs how a Hanafi mufti should issue fatwas. It declares that the mufti is obligated (wajib) to follow either the opinion that has been determined as preponderant (rajih) by the scholars of tarjih, or, if no such determination exists, the ruling as recorded in the most authoritative layer of Hanafi transmission: Zahir al-Riwayah. This establishes a clear hierarchy of authority, preventing arbitrary selection of opinions. The Urdu commentary reinforces that the mufti must not exercise personal preference but adhere to the established tarjih of the school. Understanding this rule is foundational for anyone who aspires to issue or evaluate Hanafi fatwas.

Guided Path
1
Identify the obligation
Extract the central command: the mufti is obligated (wajib) to follow a specific type of ruling. Which ruling? Either the one whose preponderance is known from its people, or one from Zahir al-Riwayah when no contrary tarjih exists.
The term 'wajib' indicates a legal obligation, not just recommendation.
2
Understand the two sources of authority
The first source is a ruling that has been explicitly declared rajih by the ahl al-tarjih (scholars of preponderance). The second is a ruling recorded in Zahir al-Riwayah (the six core books of Imam Muhammad) provided that the ahl al-tarjih have not given preponderance to a contrary ruling. These two sources exhaust the mufti's permissible basis.
Zahir al-Riwayah is the highest authority in the school; only if there is clear tarjih to the contrary can it be set aside.
3
Recognize the role of ahl al-tarjih
The passage states that tarjih is performed by 'its people' – the qualified scholars of the school. The mufti does not perform his own tarjih; he follows the determinations made by these earlier authorities. The Urdu verses clarify that only one class (ahl al-tarjih) can give preponderance, and their decision must be accepted.
This prevents subjective choice and anchors the mufti's practice in the collective scholarly legacy.

اعْلَمُ بأن الواجب اتِّبَاعُ مَا تَرْجِيْحُهُ عَنْ أَهْلِهِ قَدْ عُلِمَا ۹ أوْ كانَ ظَاهِرَ الرِّوَايَةِ وَلَمْ ١٠ يُرَجُحُوا خِلافَ ذَاكَ فَاعْلَمُ ترجمہ: جان لو! کہ اس قول کی پیروی کرنا واجب ہے جس کا راج ہونا اصحاب الترجیح سے معلوم ہو چکا ہو، یا وہ مسئلہ ظاہر الروایہ میں مذکور ہو اور اصحاب الترجیح نے اس کے خلاف دوسرے قول کو راجح نہ قرار دیا ہو، پس اس سے آگاہ ہو جاؤ۔ بات دل نشیں ہوں سب جان لیں ضروری جو حکم دیں ہو راجح ہو پیروی اسی کی ترجیح بھی انھیں کی جو اس کے اہل ٹھہریں ہیں کون اہل اس کے یہ بھی یہیں سمجھ لیں ان میں سے ایک طبقہ ترجیح دے سکے گا جو فیصلہ ہو اس کا وہ ماننا پڑے گا
Know that it is obligatory to follow that which its preponderance from its people is known, or it is from Zahir al-Riwayah and they did not preponderate the contrary of that, so know. Translation: Know! That it is obligatory to follow that statement whose preponderance is known from the people of tarjih, or that issue is mentioned in Zahir al-Riwayah and the people of tarjih have not declared the other statement as preponderant over it, so be aware of this. Let the words be heart-pleasing; everyone must know the necessary; whatever ruling is given that is rajih, follow it. And the tarjih is only for those who are qualified for it; who is qualified for it, understand this here. One class among them can give tarjih; whatever decision he makes must be accepted.
Source pages p. 26

Commentary
This passage is a rasm al-mufti rule. It states that a mufti is obligated to give fatwa according to the rajih (preponderant) opinion within the Hanafi school. The rajih opinion is determined by the people of tarjih (scholars of preponderance). Alternatively, if the issue is recorded in Zahir al-Riwayah (the six core books of Imam Muhammad al-Shaybani) and the people of tarjih have not given preponderance to a contrary opinion, then that ruling must be followed. The Urdu translation elaborates that following such an opinion is wajib (obligatory). The additional poetic lines emphasize that tarjih is done by qualified scholars and their decision must be accepted. This rule is foundational for the methodology of ifta.

Key Terms
ظاهر الرواية (Zahir al-Riwayah): The well-known transmitted rulings in the six core books of Imam Muhammad al-Shaybani
ترجیح (tarjih): The process of weighing conflicting opinions to determine the preponderant one

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to this passage, what is wajib (obligatory) for a mufti?
A) To follow his own opinion
B) To follow the rajih opinion or that from Zahir al-Riwayah
C) To follow the weaker opinion to be cautious
D) To always give fatwa according to Imam Abu Hanifa only

Key Takeaway
A mufti must not choose opinions by personal preference; he is bound to follow the preponderant opinion established by ahl al-tarjih or the authoritative Zahir al-Riwayah if no tarjih exists.

Rasm al-Mufti — Rules of Issuing Fatwa
intermediate
8 min
The Obligation to Follow the Rajih and the Six Books of Zahir al-Riwayah
Sets out the obligation to follow the preponderant opinion and defines Zahir al-Riwayah.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the obligation to follow the rajih opinion and identify the six books of Zahir al-Riwayah, distinguishing them from Nawadir and Nawazil.

Core Idea
It is wajib to follow the preponderant opinion, and the six books of Zahir al-Riwayah are the primary authoritative source for fatwa.

A mufti must know which opinions are binding and from which books. This lesson establishes the duty to follow the rajih (preponderant) opinion as determined by the authorities, and introduces the Zahir al-Riwayah texts that form the core of the Hanafi school. Understanding this hierarchy is essential for correct fatwa.

Guided Path
1
Recognize the obligation to follow the rajih
The passage states it is wajib (obligatory) to follow that which has been given preponderance (tarjih) by its people (ahl al-tarjih). If the opinion is in Zahir al-Riwayah and no contrary opinion has been given preponderance, that must be followed.
The 'people of tarjih' are the competent Hanafi authorities capable of weighing evidence.
2
Identify the definition of Zahir al-Riwayah
Zahir al-Riwayah refers to the six books authored by Imam Muhammad al-Shaybani in which he compiled the Hanafi madhhab with sound chains of transmission.
These books are also called 'al-Usul' (the principles).
3
List the six books
Memorize the six: al-Jami' al-Saghir, al-Jami' al-Kabir, al-Siyar al-Saghir, al-Siyar al-Kabir, al-Ziyadat, and al-Mabsut.
They are transmitted with mutawatir chains (tawaturat bi al-sanad al-mabdut).
4
Distinguish from Nawadir and Nawazil
Nawadir are rare transmissions with weak chains; Nawazil are new cases derived by later scholars using proofs. Both are subordinate to Zahir al-Riwayah.
For fatwa, Zahir al-Riwayah takes precedence over these.
5
Apply the hierarchy in fatwa
When issuing a fatwa, first look in the six books. If no explicit ruling, then check Nawazil works like al-Fatawa al-Hindiyya, but the rajih from the six is preferred even over alternate opinions unless tarjih is given.
This consensus rule ensures consistency in the school.

وَكُتُبُ ظَاهِرِ الرِّوَايَات أَتَتْ سِتًّا وَبِالأَصُولِ أيضاً سُمِّيَتُ صَنَّفها محمَّدُ الشَّيْبَانِيُّ ۱۲ حَرَّرَ فِيهَا الْمَذْهَبَ النُّعْمَانِي الْجَامِعُ الصَّغِيرُ وَ الْكَثِيرُ ١٣ وَ السِّيرُ الكبيرُ وَالصَّغِيرُ ثُمَّ الزِّيَادَاتُ مَعَ الْمَبْسُوط ۱۴ تَوَاتَرَتْ بِالسَّنَدِ الْمَضْبُوط كَذَا لَهُ مَسَائِلُ النَّوَادِرُ ۱۵ إِسْنَادُهَا فِي الْكُتُبِ غَيْرُ ظَاهِرُ وَبَعْدَهَا مَسَائِلُ النَّوَازِلُ ١٦ خَرَّجَهَا الْأَشْيَاحُ بِالدَّلَائِلُ ترجمہ: اور ظاہر الروایہ کی کتابیں چھ ہیں جن کا نام الاصول بھی رکھا گیا ہے، امام محمد الشیبانی نے ان کتابوں کو لکھا ہے جن میں امام اعظم کے مذہب کو بیان کیا ہے، (وہ کتابیں) جامع صغیر، جامع کبیر، سیر کبیر اور سیر صغیر ہیں، پھر مبسوط اور زیادات ہیں، یہ مضبوط سند سے مروی ہیں، اسی طرح امام محمد کی کتابوں میں نوادر کے مسائل بھی ہیں جن کی سندیں مشہور نہیں ہیں اور نوادر کے بعد نوازل کے مسائل ہیں جن کو مشائخ نے دلائل کے ساتھ مستنبط کیا ہے۔ فتوے میں ہیں مقدم کل چھ کتابیں سنئے یا چاہے آپ ان کو اصل الاصول کہئے معروف نام ہے ان کا ظاہر الروایہ استاذ نے ہمیں تو عنوان یہی بتایا ترجیح میں مقدم ہے ظاہر الروایہ اجماعی قاعدہ ہے یہ ماننا پڑے گا اور ظاہر روایت مفتی بہ ہے تب بھی دوسرا بھی ترجیح سے ہو خالی جب قول اور ظاہر روایت پر مشتمل کتابیں تعداد ان کی چھ ہے بس ان کو آپ مانیں
Know that it is obligatory to follow that which has been given preponderance by its people (the authorities), or that which is in Zahir al-Riwayah, and they have not given preponderance to something else. So know that. (Urdu: Understand that it is obligatory to follow the saying whose preponderance has been established by the people of tarjih, or the issue is mentioned in Zahir al-Riwayah and the people of tarjih have not given preponderance to another saying. So be aware of this. The words should be heart-pleasing, everyone should know that whatever ruling is given must be rajih; follow it. Preponderance also belongs to those who are its people. Who are its people, also understand here. One class among them can give preponderance; whatever decision they make must be accepted.) And the books of Zahir al-Riwayah came as six, and they are also called al-Usul. Muhammad al-Shaybani authored them; he compiled in them the Nu'mani (Hanafi) madhhab. They are al-Jami' al-Saghir, al-Jami' al-Kabir, al-Siyar al-Kabir, al-Siyar al-Saghir, then al-Ziyadat along with al-Mabsut. They are transmitted with a sound chain. Also, he has Nawadir issues whose chains in the books are not apparent. After them are Nawazil issues which the mashayikh derived with proofs. (Urdu: And the books of Zahir al-Riwayah are six, also named al-Usul. Imam Muhammad al-Shaybani wrote these books in which he explained the madhhab of Imam A'zam. The books are: al-Jami' al-Saghir, al-Jami' al-Kabir, al-Siyar al-Kabir, al-Siyar al-Saghir, then al-Mabsut and al-Ziyadat. They are transmitted with a strong chain. Similarly, Imam Muhammad's books also contain Nawadir issues whose chains are not well-known. After Nawadir are Nawazil issues which the mashayikh derived with proofs. In fatwa, the six books are given precedence; you may call them Usul al-Usul. Their well-known name is Zahir al-Riwayah; our teacher gave us this title. In preponderance, Zahir al-Riwayah is given precedence; this is a consensus rule that must be accepted. Also, the Zahir al-Riwayah is the fatwa basis even when there is a second saying without preponderance. And the books containing Zahir al-Riwayah are six; only these you must accept.)
Source pages p. 26

Commentary
This passage is a rule of fatwa (rasm al-mufti) establishing the hierarchy of sources the mufti must follow. It opens by stating that it is obligatory (wajib) to follow the preponderant (rajih) opinion as determined by the authorized scholars (ahl al-tarjih). If a ruling is found in Zahir al-Riwayah and no other opinion has been given preponderance by the scholars, then that is the one to follow. The author then identifies the six books of Zahir al-Riwayah authored by Imam Muhammad al-Shaybani: al-Jami' al-Saghir, al-Jami' al-Kabir, al-Siyar al-Kabir, al-Siyar al-Saghir, al-Ziyadat, and al-Mabsut. These are also called 'al-Usul' and are transmitted with mutawatir chains. After these, there are the Nawadir (rare transmissions) with weak chains, and then the Nawazil (new cases) which later scholars derived using proofs. The Urdu commentary reinforces that these six books are the foundation of fatwa and that their precedence is a matter of consensus.

Key Terms
ظاهر الرواية (Zahir al-Riwayah): The six books authored by Imam Muhammad al-Shaybani that contain the primary, well-known transmissions of the Hanafi school, transmitted with sound chains.
الاصول (al-Usul): Another name for the six books of Zahir al-Riwayah.
نوادر (Nawadir): Rare transmissions from Imam Muhammad or other early Hanafi scholars, with chains that are not widely known or established.
نوازل (Nawazil): New cases and issues derived by later Hanafi scholars (mashayikh) using proofs and ijtihad, after the time of the early authorities.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Key Takeaway
It is wajib to follow the rajih opinion, and the six books of Zahir al-Riwayah are the primary authoritative sources for fatwa in the Hanafi school.

Rasm al-Mufti — Rules of Issuing Fatwa
intermediate
8 min
Hierarchy of Hanafi Sources: Zahir al-Riwayah, Nawadir, Nawazil
Rasm al-mufti rule establishing source hierarchy

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the six books of Zahir al-Riwayah, explain their precedence over Nawadir and Nawazil, and apply the hierarchy when issuing a fatwa.

Core Idea
Zahir al-Riwayah is the bedrock of Hanafi fatwa: its six books take precedence over all later transmissions.

The Hanafi school has a clear hierarchy of authoritative texts. Before issuing a fatwa, a mufti must know which sources to consult first. This lesson outlines that hierarchy, distinguishing between the core transmissions of Imam Muhammad (Zahir al-Riwayah), rare transmissions (Nawadir), and later derived issues (Nawazil).

Guided Path
1
Enumerate the six books of Zahir al-Riwayah
List the six books authored by Imam Muhammad al-Shaybani: al-Jami' al-Saghir, al-Jami' al-Kabir, al-Siyar al-Kabir, al-Siyar al-Saghir, al-Ziyadat, and al-Mabsut.
These are also called al-Usul (the fundamentals).
2
Understand why they are called al-Usul
These books contain the well-known transmitted rulings of the early Hanafi school, transmitted through strong chains (tawatur). They form the foundation of the madhhab.
The term Zahir al-Riwayah distinguishes them from later, less-known rulings.
3
Distinguish Nawadir and Nawazil
Nawadir are rare transmissions in Imam Muhammad's books with chains that are not apparent (ghayr zahir). Nawazil are later issues derived by scholars (mashayikh) using evidence.
Nawadir have lower authority than Zahir al-Riwayah; Nawazil are consulted only after the primary sources.
4
Apply the preferential ruling
In issuing a fatwa, the mufti must give precedence to Zahir al-Riwayah over Nawadir and Nawazil. This is a consensus rule (ijma'i qa'idah).
Exception: if the six books are silent, or if later custom necessitates, Nawazil may be considered.
5
Note the internal hierarchy
Among the six, al-Mabsut is known as al-Asl because it was composed first. The last composed is al-Siyar al-Kabir, which is the most relied-upon (mu'tamad).
Al-Jami' al-Saghir is based on al-Asl and also takes precedence.

Source Text

وَكُتُبُ ظَاهِرِ الرِّوَايَات أَتَتْ سِئًا وَبِالأَصُولِ أيضاً سُمِّيَتُ صَنَّفها محمَّدُ الشَّيْبَانِيُّ ۱۲ حَرَّرَ فِيهَا الْمَذْهَبَ النُّعْمَانِي الْجَامِعُ الصَّغِيرُ وَ الْكَثِيرُ ١٣ وَ السِّيرُ الكبيرُ وَالصَّغِيرُ ثُمَّ الزِّيَادَاتُ مَعَ الْمَبْسُوط ۱۴ تَوَاتَرَتْ بِالسَّنَدِ الْمَضْبُوط كَذَا لَهُ مَسَائِلُ النَّوَادِرُ ۱۵ إِسْنَادُهَا فِي الْكُتُبِ غَيْرُ ظَاهِرُ وَبَعْدَهَا مَسَائِلُ النَّوَازِلُ ١٦ خَرَّجَهَا الْأَشْيَاحُ بِالدَّلَائِلُ ترجمہ: اور ظاہر الروایہ کی کتابیں چھ ہیں جن کا نام الاصول بھی رکھا گیا ہے، امام محمد الشیبانی نے ان کتابوں کو لکھا ہے جن میں امام اعظم کے مذہب کو بیان کیا ہے، ( وہ کتابیں) جامع صغیر، جامع کبیر، سیر کبیر اور سیر صغیر ہیں، پھر مبسوط اور زیادات ہیں، یہ مضبوط سند سے مروی ہیں، اسی طرح امام محمد کی کتابوں میں نوادر کے مسائل بھی ہیں جن کی سندیں مشہور نہیں ہیں اور نوادر کے بعد نوازل کے مسائل ہیں جن کو مشائخ نے دلائل کے ساتھ مستنبط کیا ہے۔ فتوے میں ہیں مقدم کل چھ کتابیں سنئے
یا چاہے آپ ان کو اصل الاصول کہئے
معروف نام ہے ان کا ظاہر الروایہ استاذ نے ہمیں تو عنوان یہی بتایا

وَاشْتَهَرَ الْمَبْسُوطُ بِالأَصْلِ، وَذَا ١٧ لِسَبْقِهِ السِّتَّةِ تَصْنِيفًا كَذَا الْجَامِعُ الصَّغِيرُ بَعْدَهُ فَمَا ١٨ فِيهِ عَلَى الْأَصْلِ لِذَا تَقَدَّمَا

And the books of Zahir al-Riwayah are six, and they are also called al-Usul. Muhammad al-Shaybani authored them, in which he recorded the Nu'mani madhhab. They are: al-Jami' al-Saghir, al-Jami' al-Kabir, al-Siyar al-Kabir, al-Siyar al-Saghir, then al-Ziyadat and al-Mabsut. These were transmitted through a strong chain of transmission. Likewise, he has the Masail al-Nawadir, whose chains in the books are not apparent. After them are the Masail al-Nawazil, which the teachers derived with proofs. Translation: And the books of Zahir al-Riwayah are six, which are also named al-Usul. Imam Muhammad al-Shaybani wrote these books in which he explained the madhhab of Imam A'zam. (Those books are) Jami' Saghir, Jami' Kabir, Siyar Kabir, and Siyar Saghir, then Mabsut and Ziyadat. They are narrated with strong chains. Similarly, in Imam Muhammad's books there are also Nawadir issues whose chains are not well known. After Nawadir are the Nawazil issues which the scholars derived with proofs. In fatwa, the six books are given precedence. Or you can call them Asl al-Usul. Their well-known name is Zahir al-Riwayah; my teacher taught me this very title. In preference, Zahir al-Riwayah is given precedence; this is a consensus rule that must be accepted. And the mufti works with Zahir al-Riwayah even if another opinion exists when it is [؟] empty. And the books comprising Zahir al-Riwayah are six; just accept them. And al-Mabsut became famous as al-Asl, because it preceded the six in composition. Likewise, al-Jami' al-Saghir came after it, and what is in it is based on al-Asl, so it also takes precedence. The last of the six in composition is al-Siyar al-Kabir, so it is the relied-upon [book]. Translation: And al-Mabsut is famous as al-Asl because in composition it preceded the six. Likewise, al-Jami' al-Saghir came after it, so what is in it follows al-Asl, thus it also takes precedence. The last of the six in composition is al-Siyar al-Kabir, so it is the relied-upon [book].
Source pages p. 27

Commentary
This passage is a rasm al-mufti rule that establishes the hierarchy of authoritative texts in the Hanafi school for issuing fatwas. The author, from the book Naseem Ul Mufti (a commentary on 'Uqud Rasm al-Mufti), enumerates the six books of Zahir al-Riwayah authored by Imam Muhammad al-Shaybani: al-Jami' al-Saghir, al-Jami' al-Kabir, al-Siyar al-Kabir, al-Siyar al-Saghir, al-Ziyadat, and al-Mabsut. These are called al-Usul or the primary sources. They form the foundation of the madhhab because they contain the well-known transmitted positions of Imam Abu Hanifa, Abu Yusuf, and Muhammad. The passage then notes that beside these, there are Nawadir (rare transmissions) with weaker chains, and after those, Nawazil (later juristic issues) derived by later scholars. The rule established is that in giving fatwa, the six books of Zahir al-Riwayah are given precedence (muqaddam) over Nawadir and Nawazil. This is a consensus rule. The passage also clarifies a secondary point: al-Mabsut is called al-Asl because it was composed first (even though it is one of the six), and al-Jami' al-Saghir follows it in authority. The last composed of the six is al-Siyar al-Kabir, which is therefore the mu'tamad (reliable version) for later scholarship. This hierarchy ensures that the mufti first consults the core transmissions before rare or later opinions.

Key Terms
ظاهر الرواية (Zāhir al-Riwāyah): The six books authored by Imam Muhammad al-Shaybani that contain the well-known transmitted rulings of the early Hanafi school, transmitted through strong chains.
النوادر (al-Nawādir): Rare transmissions within Imam Muhammad’s books that are not as widely known as the Zahir al-Riwayah; their chains are not apparent (ghayr zahir).
النوازل (al-Nawāzil): Later juristic issues derived by later scholars (mashayikh) based on evidence; they are not part of the original transmissions of Imam Muhammad.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Which book of Zahir al-Riwayah is considered the last in composition and therefore the most reliable (mu'tamad)?
A) Al-Mabsut
B) Al-Jami' al-Saghir
C) Al-Siyar al-Kabir
D) Al-Ziyadat

Key Takeaway
The six books of Zahir al-Riwayah are the primary source for Hanafi fatwa, and their rulings are preferred over Nawadir and Nawazil.

Hierarchy of Narration from Abu Hanifa and His Disciples
Rasm al-mufti rule establishing hierarchy of narrations

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to construct the hierarchy of narrations within the Hanafi school for issuing fatwas.

Core Idea
When Imam Abu Hanifa's own choice is absent, the mufti follows Abu Yusuf, then Muhammad, then Zufar and Ibn Ziyad.

This lesson presents a foundational rule (rasm al-mufti) that every Hanafi mufti must know: the precise order of authority among narrations from the Imam and his students. Understanding this hierarchy ensures your fatwa aligns with the school's most reliable positions.

Guided Path
1
Identify the primary source
Recognize that all valid narrations ultimately go back to Imam Abu Hanifa's own selection (ikhtiyar). No opinion of any other scholar is taken over his.
The text states: 'there is no statement for others besides him'.
2
Identify the first successor
When Abu Hanifa's ikhtiyar is not recorded, the next authority is Imam Abu Yusuf (Ya'qub). His opinion is called 'al-mukhtar' (the chosen).
The text says: 'then the statement of Ya'qub is the chosen one'.
3
Identify the second successor
If neither Abu Hanifa nor Abu Yusuf's views are found, then the opinion of Imam Muhammad al-Shaybani is taken. His is described as 'al-hasan' (the good).
The text says: 'then Muhammad's statement is the good one'.
4
Identify the third successor
Finally, if the above are absent, the opinions of Zufar and Hasan ibn Ziyad are considered, also described as 'al-hasan' (the good).
The text says: 'then Zufar and Ibn Ziyad are the good ones'.
5
Apply the hierarchy
In practice, the mufti must follow this order strictly, never skipping a level unless the higher authority's opinion is truly unknown or not transmitted.
This rule ensures consistency and reliability in Hanafi fatwa practice.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وَاعْلَمْ بِأَنَّ عَنْ أَبِي حَنِيفَةَ جَاءَتْ رِوَايَاتٌ غَدَتْ مُنِيفَةٌ اخْتَارَ مِنْهَا بَعْضَهَا وَالْبَاقِي يَخْتَارُ مِنْهُ سَائِرُ الرِّفَاقِ فَلَمْ يَكُنْ لِغَيْرِهِ جَوَابٌ كَمَا عَلَيْهِ أَقْسَمَ الْأَصْحَابُ وَحَيْثُ لَمْ يُوجَدْ لَهُ اخْتِيَارُ فَقَوْلُ يَعْقُوبَ هُوَ الْمُخْتَارُ ثُمَّ مُحَمَّدٍ فَقَوْلُهُ الْحَسَنُ ثُمَّ زُفَرَ وَابْنِ زِيَادٍ الْحَسَنُ
And know that from Abu Hanifa came many abundant narrations; he selected some of them, and the remaining are selected from by the rest of the companions. So there is no statement for others besides him, as the companions swore upon that. And when his choice is not found, then the statement of Ya'qub (Abu Yusuf) is the chosen one, then Muhammad's statement is the good one, then Zufar and Ibn Ziyad are the good ones.
Source pages p. 30

Commentary
This is a methodological rule (rasm al-mufti) presenting the hierarchy of authorities within the Hanafi school. It first affirms that all valid narrations in the school ultimately go back to Imam Abu Hanifa, as his direct students (the Sahibayn and others) swore that. This establishes Imam Abu Hanifa as the primary source. Then, it provides the order of precedence when the Imam's own chosen opinion (ikhtiyar) is not recorded for a particular issue: first, the opinion of Abu Yusuf; second, that of Imam Muhammad al-Shaybani; third, those of Zufar and Hasan ibn Ziyad. This hierarchy is critical for muftis and judges to follow in issuing fatwas, ensuring consistency with the school's transmitted positions.

Key Terms
اختيار (ikhtiyar): The opinion chosen by Imam Abu Hanifa himself from among multiple narrations
صاحبين (sahibayn): Abu Yusuf and Muhammad al-Shaybani, the two foremost students of Abu Hanifa

Key Terms from Source Passage

B) Imam Abu Yusuf
C) Imam Zufar
D) Hasan ibn Ziyad

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi mufti must follow the prescribed hierarchy of narrations: Abu Hanifa first, then Abu Yusuf, then Muhammad, then Zufar and Ibn Ziyad.

Rasm al-Mufti — Rules of Issuing Fatwa

Tarjih Rules When Imam and Sahibayn Differ
Rasm al-Mufti rule on resolving Imam-Sahibayn conflicts

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the three approaches (takhyir, tarjih bi al-dalil, tafsil) and apply the later Hanafi rule that a mufti must follow the preponderated views of the mutakhkhirin.

Core Idea
When Imam and Sahibayn differ, the mufti today must follow later scholars' tarjih, not his own judgment.

In Hanafi fatwa practice, a classic dilemma arises when Imam Abu Hanifa's opinion contradicts that of his two main students, Abu Yusuf and Muhammad al-Shaybani. Early Hanafis proposed three methods to resolve this: free choice, evidence weighing, and the detailed approach. This lesson unpacks each method and explains why the later school settled on a practical hierarchy, making it essential for a mufti to rely on the tarjih compiled by authoritative late scholars.

Guided Path
1
Identify the three approaches
List the three methods mentioned in the text: takhyir (free choice), tarjih bi al-dalil (weighing evidence), and tafsil (detailed approach).
These correspond to three historical positions within the Hanafi school.
2
Determine who each method applies to
Note that tarjih bi al-dalil is valid only for a mufti possessing ijtihad, while takhyir and tafsil are for those without. Identify which method is deemed 'most correct' according to the text.
The text says tarjih bi al-dalil is 'most correct for a mufti possessing ijtihad' but then declares it inapplicable today.
3
Explain why tarjih bi al-dalil is no longer viable
The text states: 'الآن لا ترجيح بالدليل' — now there is no weighing by evidence. Understand that this reflects the mutakhkhirin view that later muftis lack the capacity to evaluate evidence directly.
This is a pragmatic closure: the school's later authorities have already done the weighing.
4
Describe the tafsil approach
The tafsil method means following what later scholars (ashab al-tarjih) have preponderated. The text says 'take that which has become clear to them' and mentions Zufar's opinion weighed in seventeen issues as an example.
The mufti's duty is to consult authoritative fatwa works, not to re-weigh evidence.
5
Apply the rule: what should a mufti do today?
When faced with a conflict between Imam and Sahibayn, the mufti must look up the issue in reliable late Hanafi texts (e.g., al-Hidayah, al-Durr al-Mukhtar) and follow the view that later scholars have marked as rajih (preponderant).
This ensures consistency and prevents chaotic ijtihad.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وَقِيلَ بِالتَّخْيِيرِ فِي فَتَوَاهُ إِنْ خَالَفَ الْإِمَامَ صَاحِبَاهُ وَقِيلَ مَنْ دَلِيلُهُ أَقْوَى رَجَحَ وَذَا لِمُفْتٍ ذِي اجْتِهَادٍ الْأَصَحُ فَالْآنَ لَا تَرْجِيحَ بِالدَّلِيلِ فَلَيْسَ إِلَّا الْقَوْلُ بِالتَّفْصِيلِ مَا لَمْ يَكُنْ خِلَافُهُ الْمُصَحَّحَا فَنَأْخُذُ الَّذِي لَهُمْ قَدْ وَضَحَا فَإِنَّنَا نَرَاهُمْ قَدْ رَجَّحُوا مَقَالَ بَعْضِ صَحْبِهِ وَصَحَّحُوا مِنْ ذَاكَ مَا قَدْ رَجَّحُوا لِزُفَرَ مَقَالَهُ فِي سَبْعَةٍ وَعَشَرَ
And it was said: there is option (takhyir) in his fatwa when the two companions disagree with the Imam. And it was said: whoever's evidence is stronger prevails, and this is most correct for a mufti possessing ijtihad. But now there is no weighing by evidence; the only thing is the detailed approach (tafsil) as long as its disagreement is not corrected. So we take that which has become clear to them. Indeed, we see that they have given weight to the statement of some of his companions and corrected from that what they gave weight to for Zufar: his statement in seventeen matters.
Source pages p. 31

Commentary
This passage is a rasm al-mufti rule addressing a classic Hanafi internal dispute: what should a mufti do when Imam Abu Hanifa's opinion conflicts with that of his two main students (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad)? Three views are presented: first, the mufti has free choice (takhyir) between them; second, the mufti should weigh the evidence (tarjih bi al-dalil) and follow the stronger proof, but this applies only to a mufti capable of ijtihad; third, the relied-upon view for later times (mutakhkhirun) is the 'detailed approach' (al-qawl bi al-tafsil), which means that one should follow what the later authoritative scholars (the 'redactors' or 'ashab al-tarjih') have preponderated (rajjahu) in their works, rather than attempting independent evidence evaluation. The passage then supports this by noting that these later scholars have, for example, given preponderance to Imam Zufar's opinion in seventeen specific issues. Thus, the mufti's duty is to follow the weightings established by the later school authorities, not to engage in fresh ijtihad.

Key Terms
تخيير (takhyir): Free choice given to the mufti between conflicting opinions of Imam and Sahibayn
ترجيح بالدليل (tarjih bi al-dalil): Weighing opinions based on the strength of their evidence (Quran, hadith, qiyas)
تفصيل (tafsil): The detailed approach of following the preponderated opinions recorded by later authoritative scholars
مفتٍ ذو اجتهاد (muftin dhu ijtihad): A mufti capable of independent legal reasoning (ijtihad)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

B) Weigh the evidence himself
C) Follow the opinion that later scholars have made preponderant
D) Select the opinion of the greater number of scholars

Key Takeaway
A Hanafi mufti must follow the tarjih decisions of later scholars (tafsil) rather than independently weighing evidence when Imam and Sahibayn differ.

8 min
Tarjih Rules for Mufti When Imam and Sahibayn Differ
Explains the three views on mufti's choice when Abu Hanifa's opinion contradicts the two companions.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the three views (takhyeer, tarjih bi al-dalil, tafsil) and apply the preferred view (tafsil) when a mufti faces a conflict between Imam Abu Hanifa and the Sahibayn.

Core Idea
A non-mujtahid mufti must follow the Sahibayn unless the Imam's view is authenticated.

When Imam Abu Hanifa and his two companions disagree, which opinion should a mufti give? Some say the mufti may choose freely; others say he must weigh evidence; the Hanafi tarjih tradition settles on a third path: rely on the Sahibayn unless later scholars have authenticated the Imam's view. This lesson examines these three views and their practical implications for fatwa.

Guided Path
1
Identify the three views
Extract from the passage the three views: takhyeer (choice), tarjih bi al-dalil (weighting by evidence), and tafsil (detailed view).
These are marked in the Arabic verses with 'wa qila' and 'wa dha li mufti dhi ijtihad'.
2
Understand the scope of each view
Determine who can apply each view: takhyeer is open to any; tarjih bi al-dalil is for mujtahids; tafsil is for muqallid muftis.
The passage says 'wa dha li mufti dhi ijtihad' and 'fa al-ana la tarjih bi al-dalil'.
3
Identify the preferred view and its condition
The passage states that the detailed view (tafsil) is the relied-upon position: follow the Sahibayn unless the Imam's view has been authenticated (musahhah).
The key line: 'ma lam yakun khilafuhu al-musahhaha'.
4
Recognize the example of Zufar's 17 issues
The passage mentions that later scholars authenticated Zufar's view in 17 issues, demonstrating the practice of tashih (authentication).
This shows that even a junior companion's view can be preferred when authenticated.
5
Apply the rule to a scenario
Given a concrete conflict between Imam and Sahibayn, decide which opinion the mufti should give, checking for authentication.
Use the fatwa hierarchy to justify your decision.

ہوگی وہی قول رائج ہوگا، اور یہ قول مجتہد مفتی کے لئے زیادہ صحیح ہے، پس اب دلیل کی بناء پر ترجیح کی بات نہیں رہی، بلکہ تفصیل والا قول ہی درست ہے، جب تک کہ امام صاحب کے علاوہ قول کی تصیح نہ کی گئی ہو، تو ہم اس قول کو لے لیں گے جو اصحاب الترجیح کے لئے واضح ہوا ہے، کیوں کہ ہم انھیں دیکھتے ہیں کہ انھوں نے امام صاحب کے بعض شاگردوں کے قول کو راجح قرار دیا ہے، اسی میں سے ہے جو انھوں نے سترہ مسائل میں Naseem al-Mufti in the solution of [؟] The chosen opinion will be. When the statement of Imam A'zam (Abu Hanifa) is not found, then (Imam) Ya'qub (Abu Yusuf) said that which to make mufta bih. [Then] after them, place the number of Ibn al-Hasan (Muhammad al-Shaybani), then at the end Zufar, and Ibn Ziyad also. And it was said: the mufti has choice in his fatwa (28) when the two companions differ from the Imam. And it was said: the one whose evidence is stronger is given weight (29) and this is for a mufti who has independent reasoning, but that is correct. Now there is no weighting by evidence (30), so there is nothing but the detailed view, as long as the opposite isn't correct. So we take that which has become clear to them (the scholars of tarjih). And we see that they have given weight (32) to the statement of some of his (Abu Hanifa's) companions, and they have authenticated from that which they gave weight to for Zufar (33) his statement in seventeen issues. Translation: And it was said that the mufti will have choice in giving fatwa when the Imam and the two companions differ. And another view is that the one whose evidence is stronger will prevail. And this view is more correct for a mujtahid mufti. So now weighting by evidence is not possible; rather the detailed view is correct, as long as authentication of the opposite (of the Imam's view) has not been done. So we take that view which has become clear to the scholars of tarjih. Because we see that they have given weight to the statement of some of his companions, and from that which they gave weight to for Zufar, his statement in seventeen issues. The order is transmitted in books with 'qila' (it was said), but there are also two other views. In one view, the mufti may choose whatever he wants: if there is only the Imam's statement, he may give fatwa on it; or he may give fatwa on the companions' statement. In the second view, the mufti looks at the evidence and adopts the stronger one. Not every mufti can do this; it is a decision that only a mujtahid can make. Now the muftis are transmitters, not mujtahids. They do not see the evidence. The detailed view passed earlier: as long as the Imam's statement is not authenticated (musahhah), the mufti must take it. If the opposite is authenticated, then we also say that fatwa is better upon that. Those who have the right of tarjih, they keep an eye on the students; they give tarjih. There are seventeen issues in which Zufar is the source, all these issues are strong in evidence. Those who have the right of tarjih will give tarjih; we are all muqallids, we will follow.
Source pages p. 31

Commentary
This passage from Naseem Ul Mufti explains the methodological rule (rasm al-mufti) regarding how a Hanafi mufti should issue fatwas when there is disagreement between Imam Abu Hanifa and his two main companions, Abu Yusuf and Muhammad al-Shaybani. The author presents three views found in the later scholarship: (1) The mufti has absolute choice (takhyeer) to follow either the Imam or the Sahibayn as he wishes. (2) The mufti must weigh the evidence (tarjih bi al-dalil) and follow the stronger proof. (3) The preferred view (al-qaul bi al-tafsil) is that the mufti should follow the opinion of the Sahibayn over that of the Imam, unless the Imam's view has been explicitly authenticated (musahhah) by later scholars. The passage explains that the first two views are not practical for contemporary non-mujtahid muftis. Since they are not capable of weighing evidence, they must rely on the work of earlier mujtahids who have determined which opinions are stronger. The author notes that in 17 specific issues, the opinion of Imam Zufar (another student of Abu Hanifa) has been preferred. This rule ensures consistency in fatwa and prevents unqualified muftis from exercising personal judgment. The final line underscores that the mufti of today is a mere transmitter (naqil) and must follow the established tarjih of the scholars.

Key Terms
تخيير (takhyeer): The view that a mufti may choose between conflicting opinions of Imam and Sahibayn as he wishes.
ترجيح بالدليل (tarjih bi al-dalil): Weighting by evidence: the mufti compares the proofs and adopts the stronger one.
تفصيل (tafsil): The detailed view: the mufti follows the Sahibayn unless the Imam's opinion has been authenticated by later scholars.
مصحح (musahhah): An opinion that has been explicitly authenticated and preferred by later Hanafi tarjih scholars.

Key Terms from Source Passage

A) Follow whichever opinion he thinks is stronger.

Key Takeaway
The mufti must follow the Sahibayn unless the Imam's view is authenticated; this ensures fatwa consistency in the Hanafi school.

Rasm al-Mufti — Rules of Issuing Fatwa
beginner
6 min
The Fatwa Hierarchy in the Absence of Transmitted Opinions
State the hierarchy of fatwa sources when riwaya absent

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify and apply the Hanafi fatwa hierarchy when no riwaya exists from early authorities, following the rule of preferring the majority of later scholars.

Core Idea
When no riwaya exists, the majority of later authorities guide the fatwa, not personal opinion.

This lesson presents a critical rule in Hanafi ifta' methodology: what should a mufti do when there is no transmitted opinion from the early masters? The text provides a clear hierarchy: first check the majority view of later scholars, and only as a last resort, employ ijtihad. This protects the mufti from reckless legal pronouncements.

Guided Path
1
Identify the gap
Recognize that no riwaya (transmitted opinion) is available from the early authoritative scholars (ashab al-darayah) like Abu Hanifa, Abu Yusuf, Muhammad, and Zufar.
This is the first condition for moving to the later hierarchy.
2
List the later authorities
Identify the later scholars (mutakhkhirin) mentioned: al-Tahawi, Abu Hafs al-Kabir, Abu Ja'far, and al-Layth al-Shahir (Abu al-Layth al-Samarqandi). Their opinions are authoritative.
These are key figures in the Hanafi school whose views carry weight.
3
Apply the majority rule
If the later scholars differ among themselves, give preference (tarjih) to the opinion held by the majority of them.
The Arabic phrase 'يُرَجَّحُ الَّذِي عَلَيْهِ الْأَكْثَرُ' establishes this majority principle.
4
Recognize the last resort
If no opinion is found from these later authorities, and a fatwa is needed, the mufti must engage in ijtihad with utmost seriousness and fear of Allah.
Ijtihad is only permissible after exhausting all transmitted sources.
5
Internalize the adab
Understand the grave responsibility of issuing fatwa; only a wretched person dares to pronounce without proper knowledge.
This adab (etiquette) is part of the rule to prevent recklessness.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
