Preference Among Conflicting Opinions and the Role of 'Urf
Explaining preference rules for conflicting opinions and the role of custom.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the rule for preferring conflicting opinions using murajjih factors, and determine when custom ('urf) can be used as a basis for rulings.

Core Idea
Resolve conflicts with murajjih; use custom only when no explicit text contradicts it.

This passage from Naseem al-Mufti equips the mufti with two vital tools: how to choose between conflicting transmitted opinions when no clear authentication exists, and how to properly invoke customary practice without overriding explicit texts. These rules ensure consistency and fidelity to the Hanafi madhhab.

Guided Path
1
Identify the conflict or absence of text
Determine if there is a contradiction in authentication (ta'ārada al-taṣḥīḥ) between two narrations, or if no explicit text exists for the issue.
The conflict can be between two qawls (opinions) or two riwayat (narrations) within the madhhab.
2
Apply the murajjih factors
If no clear tashih, select the opinion that has a preponderating factor (murajjih) from the established list (e.g., conformity with usul, ease for people, later position of the imam).
The text references ten factors detailed earlier in the work.
3
Consider custom ('urf) when no explicit text exists
If no clear text (nass) governs the case, and a customary practice is prevalent, the mufti may base the ruling on that custom, provided it does not contradict a clear, established text (sarih thabit).
This is based on the Hanafi maxim 'al-'adah muhakkamah' but with the critical condition of non-contradiction.
4
Derive the mufta bihi ruling
The operative ruling (mufta bihi) is either the preferred qawl from step 2 or the ruling derived from custom in step 3.
This is the final fatwa the mufti issues.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
سم المفتى فى حل مباد مباحث
یا ایک قول ان میں ہو ظاہر الروایہ یا پھر بڑوں نے ہم کو وہ قول ہو بتایا
شن عقود رسم المفى ا
یا قول وہ جو ٹھہرے استحسان کا نتیجہ یا وقف کے لئے ہو وہ قول فائدے کا
یا قول وہ زمانہ کے حسب حال ٹھہرے
یا قول وہ درایت میں سب سے بڑھ کے نکلے
هَذَا إِذَا تَعَارَضَ التَّصْحِيحُ ٢٦ أَوْ لَمْ يَكُنْ أَصْلاً بِهِ تَصْرِيحُ فَتَأْخُذُ الَّذِى لَهُ مُرَبِّحُ ٦٧ مِمَّا عَلِمْتَهُ، فَهَذَا الْأَوْضَحُ
ترجمہ: یہ ترجیح والی بات اس صورت میں ہے جب کہ دو اقوال کی تصحیح میں تعارض ہو جائے یا شیح کی بالکل صراحت نہ ہو، پس اس قول پر عمل کرو جس قول میں ان مرجحات میں سے کوئی وجہ ترجیح ہو جن کو آپ جان چکے ہیں، پس یہ نہایت واضح بات ہے۔ یہ جب ہے جب تعارض تصحیح میں کہیں
ہو
یا جب ہے جب کہ کوئی تصریح ہی نہیں ہو
اب آپ قول وہ لیں ترجیح جس کی دیکھیں
کل دس وجوہ ترجیح یہ تھیں جو پڑھ چکے ہیں
وَاعْمَلْ بِمَفْهُومٍ رِواياتٍ أتى ٦٨ مَالَمْ يُخالِف لِصَرِيحِ ثَبَتا وَالْعُرْقُ فِي الشَّرْعِ لَهُ اعتبار ۲۹ لِذا عَلَيْهِ الْحُكْمُ قَدْ يُدار
ترجمہ: اور آپ منقول شدہ رواجوں کے مفہوم پر عمل کریں جب تک کہ وہ کسی صریح ثابت شدہ قول کے خلاف نہ ہو، اور شریعت میں عرف کا اعتبار کیا گیا ہے، اسی وجہ سے بہت سارے احکام کا دارو مدار عرف پر رکھا گیا ہے۔
ہر قول کا مخالف مفہوم جو بھی نکلے
حکم صریح کوئی ٹکرائے جب نہ اس سے
وہ قابل عمل ہے یہ بات یاد رکھئے یہ نص میں ناروا ہے پھر اس سے باز رہئے
اور عرف کا شریعت ہے اعتبار کرتی اور حکم کا کبھی تو اس پر مدار رکھتی
This is when there is contradiction in correction (tashih) or there is no explicit text at all, then take the one that has a preponderating factor (murajjih) from what you have learned, that is the clearest. Translation: The matter of preference is when there is contradiction in the correction of two statements, or there is no explicit statement at all. Then act upon the statement that has any cause of preponderance from those preponderating factors you have already learned. That is the clearest matter. And act upon the implied meaning of transmitted customs as long as it does not contradict a clear established text, and custom ('urf) has consideration in the Shariah, therefore rulings are often based on it.
Source pages p. 41

Commentary
This passage presents a key rule in mufti methodology: when faced with conflicting transmitted opinions (such as two narrations from Imam Abu Hanifa), the mufti must first check if one has been declared sahih (authentic) in the madhhab over the other. If there is no such clear authentication, or if the texts themselves are silent on which is stronger, then the mufti should apply the well-known preponderating factors (wujuh al-tarjih). The text hints that there are ten such factors, which are detailed earlier in the work. The second part addresses custom ('urf): a mufti may rely on the common understanding and practice of the people as long as it does not contradict an explicit, established text (nass). This is based on the legal maxim 'al-'adah muhakkamah' (custom is authoritative), but with the crucial condition that custom cannot override a clear primary source. The Urdu verses summarize these rules memorably.

Key Terms
تَعَارَضَ التَّصْحِيحُ (ta'ārada al-taṣḥīḥ): contradiction in the authentication of two narrations
مُرَجِّح (murajjih): a factor that makes one opinion preponderant over another
عُرْف (ʿurf): customary practice of the people that is not based on a clear text

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the text, when should a mufti act upon the mafhum (implied meaning) of transmitted customs?
A) Always, regardless of any text.
B) When it does not contradict a clear established text.
C) Only when the custom is from the time of the Prophet.
D) Only if the custom is mentioned in a hadith.

Key Takeaway
The mufti must resolve conflicting opinions using murajjih factors and may use custom only when no explicit, established text contradicts it.

Prohibition of Weak Opinions and Exceptions
A ruling on using weak opinions in fatwa and qada with exceptions

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the general rule against using weak opinions in fatwa and qada, identify the two exceptions, and apply the stricter rule for qadis.

Core Idea
Weak opinions are forbidden in fatwa and qada, except for necessity or expert muftis; qadi's judgment is void.

This passage from Naseem al-Mufti lays down a key principle of Rasm al-Mufti: the mufti is bound by the preponderant (rajih) opinion of the Hanafi madhhab and cannot freely choose weak (da'if) opinions. However, two exceptions are recognized: genuine necessity and a mufti of well-established expertise. For qadis, the rule is even stricter—any judgment based on a weak opinion is invalid.

Guided Path
1
State the general rule
Identify the rule: لا يجوز بالضعيف العمل (it is not permissible to act upon a weak opinion). This applies to both muftis and qadis as a baseline.
The weak opinion is any opinion that is not the preponderant (rajih) one in the madhhab.
2
List the two exceptions
The text allows two exceptions: (1) a person compelled by necessity (darurah), (2) a mufti with well-known expertise (marifah mashhurah). Understand each condition.
Necessity is a strict condition; expertise must be widely recognized.
3
Distinguish the qadi's role
The qadi is explicitly excluded from these exceptions. Even if he judges by a weak opinion, his judgment does not pass (la yamdi).
Qadis are bound by the rajih of the madhhab because their decisions are binding.
4
Reflect on the rationale
The rule protects the integrity of the madhhab and prevents arbitrary departure from established rulings. The exceptions are narrow to maintain consistency.
This is a foundational rule in Hanafi iftaa methodology.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وَلا يَجُوزُ بِالضَّعِيْفِ الْعَمَل ٧٠ وَلا بِهِ يُجَابُ مَنْ جاءَ يَسْأَلْ إِلَّا لِعَامِلِ لَهُ ضَرُوْرَةً الى أو مَنْ لَهُ مَعْرِفَةٌ مَشْهُورَةً لكِنَّمَا الْقاضِي بِهِ لا يَقْضِى ٧٢ وَإِنْ قَضَى فَحُكْمُهُ لا يَمْضِى لاسِيَّما قُضاتُنا إِذْ قَيدُوا ٧٣ براجح الْمَذْهَبِ إِذْ قُلدُوا ترجمہ: اور ضعیف قول پر عمل کرنا جائز نہیں ہے اور نہ ہی ضعیف قول کے مطابق سوال کرنے والے کو جواب دیا جائے گا، مگر جس عمل کرنے والے کو مجبوری ہو یا جس مفتی کو مہارت تامہ حاصل ہو تو وہ ضعیف قول پر عمل کر سکتا ہے ) البتہ قاضی ضعیف قول کے مطابق فیصلہ نہیں کریں گے اور اگر کریں گے تو ان کا فیصلہ نافذ نہیں ہوگا، خاص طور پر ہمارے زمانے کے قاضی، اس لیے کہ انھیں مذہب کے راجح قول کے مطابق فیصلہ کرنے کا پابند بنایا گیا ہے جب انھیں قضا کا عہدہ سونپا گیا۔ قول ضعیف جو ہو اس پر عمل نہ کیجئے جو آئے پوچھنے کو وہ بے جوا. وہ بے جواب لوٹے لیکن اگر ہو کوئی مجبور بالضروره یا ایسا کوئی مفتی ہو معرفت میں شہرہ
And it is not permissible to act upon the weak opinion, nor should the one who comes asking be answered with it, except for a person compelled by necessity or for one who has well-known expertise. However, the qadi does not judge by it, and if he judges, his judgment does not pass. Especially our qadis, because they are bound by the preponderant (rajih) opinion of the madhhab when they are appointed. Translation: Acting upon a weak opinion is not permissible, nor will the questioner be answered with a weak opinion, except for someone who is compelled by necessity or a mufti who has complete expertise (well-known). However, a qadi will not judge by a weak opinion; if he judges, his judgment is not executed. Especially our qadis, because they are bound by the preponderant opinion of the madhhab when they were given the position of qada.
Source pages p. 42

Commentary
This passage establishes a crucial rule in mufti ethics: the mufti must not issue fatwas based on weak (da'if) opinions – those that are outnumbered or not fully supported by evidence in the madhhab. The default answer to a questioner must be the preponderant (rajih) view. However, there are two exceptions: firstly, a person who is in dire necessity (darurah) may act upon a weak opinion, though the mufti may not necessarily give that as a general ruling; secondly, a mufti who has achieved mastery in fiqh ('marifah mashhurah') may occasionally depart from the rajih and act upon a weak opinion if he has strong ijtihad. This is similar to the concept that an absolute mujtahid can follow his own reasoning. For qadis, the rule is stricter: they are bound by the madhhab's preponderant opinion. If a qadi issues a verdict based on a weak opinion, it is not enforceable (la yamdi). The Urdu verses reinforce that weak opinions are generally not to be used, with the exceptions of necessity and expert muftis.

Key Terms
ضَعِيْف (da'īf): an opinion that is not the preponderant (rajih) one in the madhhab
مَشْهُوْرَة (mashhūrah): well-known, famous – referring to a mufti's expertise being widely recognized
قَاضٍ (qāḍī): the judge appointed to decide disputes

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the text, what happens if a qadi gives judgment based on a weak opinion?
A) His judgment is valid if no one objects.
B) His judgment is not executed (it does not pass).
C) It is permissible if he is a knowledgeable qadi.
D) He is dismissed from his post.

Key Takeaway
A mufti must adhere to the rajih; only necessity or proven expertise allows departure; a qadi's judgment based on weak opinion is legally ineffective.

Ruling on Acting upon Weak Opinions in Fatwa
Poetic statement of the rule on weak opinions with exceptions

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the Hanafi rule on weak opinions (da'if) by identifying when it is permissible for a mufti and when it is prohibited for a judge.

Core Idea
Acting upon a weak opinion is generally prohibited, except for necessity or expert muftis; judges are strictly bound to the preponderant view.

This passage from Naseem al-Mufti lays down a fundamental rule of Hanafi fatwa procedure: the mufti must not base his response on a weak (da'if) opinion unless an exception applies. But even then, a judge (qadi) is never permitted to decide by a weak opinion. This hierarchy reflects the different responsibilities of mufti and judge.

Guided Path
1
Identify the general prohibition
State the default ruling: acting upon a weak opinion is not permissible (la yajuz).
The verse begins with this clear prohibition.
2
Identify the first exception
Recognize the exception for one who acts out of necessity (darurah) – this applies to the layperson.
The text says 'illa li'amili lahu darurah'.
3
Identify the second exception
Recognize the exception for a mufti with well-known expertise (ma'rifah mashhurah) – this applies to a qualified scholar.
The text says 'aw man lahu ma'rifah mashhurah'.
4
Identify the judge's restriction
Note that even an expert mufti acting as a judge cannot use a weak opinion; the judge is bound to the raajih (preponderant) opinion.
The verse emphasizes 'lakinama al-qadi bihi la yaqdi'.
5
Understand the rationale
Understand why judges are bound: because they are appointed under condition to follow the preponderant view of the madhhab.
The text says 'idh qayyadu biraajih al-madhhab idh qullidu'.

And acting upon a weak (opinion) is not permissible, nor is a questioner answered with it, except for one who acts due to necessity, or one who has well-known expertise. However, the judge does not judge by it, and if he judges, his ruling does not take effect. Translation: Acting upon a weak opinion is not permissible, nor should the questioner be answered according to a weak opinion, except for the one who acts out of necessity or the mufti who has complete expertise – he may act upon a weak opinion. However, the judge will not decide according to a weak opinion, and if he does, his decision will not be enforced, especially the judges of our time, because they have been bound to act according to the preponderant opinion of the madhhab when they were entrusted with the office of judgeship.
Source pages p. 42

Commentary
This passage deals with the methodological rule of issuing fatwas concerning weak (da'if) opinions within the Hanafi madhhab. The text begins by stating that acting upon a weak opinion and answering questioners with it is not permissible. This is a general prohibition. However, two exceptions follow: (1) a person who is compelled by necessity (darurah) may act upon a weak opinion for himself; (2) a mufti who has well-known expertise (ma'rifah mashhurah) may also act upon a weak opinion in his fatwa. The second exception is limited: such a mufti can give a fatwa based on a weak opinion, but a judge (qadi) cannot base his ruling on a weak opinion. If a judge does so, his judgment is not enforceable. The reason is that judges are bound to the preponderant (raajih) opinion of the madhhab, as per their appointment conditions. This distinction highlights the different levels of responsibility: a mufti may sometimes resort to a weak view in exceptional circumstances, but judicial decisions require the strongest authority within the school. The passage ends with the conclusion of the poem, praising Allah and acknowledging the poet's effort.

Key Terms
ضعیف (da'if): A weak or less authoritative opinion within the Hanafi madhhab, one that does not enjoy strong textual support or is outweighed by stronger evidence.
راجح (raajih): The preponderant or strongest opinion in the madhhab, which is the basis for fatwa and judicial decisions.
قاضی (qaadi): A judge appointed by the state to adjudicate disputes, bound in this context to the raajih opinion of the madhhab.

Key Terms from Source Passage

B) When the mufti has well-known expertise (ma'rifah mashhurah)
C) When the mufti is also a judge
D) When the mufti is asked by a friend

Key Takeaway
The rule distinguishes between mufti and judge: weak opinions are generally forbidden, but expert muftis may use them in necessity; judges are absolutely bound to the preponderant view.

Rasm al-Mufti — Rules of Issuing Fatwa

10 min
First Principle: Fatwa Upon the Rajih Opinion
States the fundamental rule of mufti's obligation to follow rajih opinion.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the raajih and marjuh opinions and apply the principle that fatwa must be based on the raajih opinion, justifying with reference to Hanafi rasm al-mufti.

Core Idea
The mufti must always follow the raajih opinion; the marjuh is prohibited except in specific exceptional cases.

This lesson introduces the foundational principle of fatwa in the Hanafi madhhab: the mufti is bound to issue rulings based on the preponderant (raajih) opinion, not on weak or outweighed (marjuh) opinions. The principle is established by scholarly consensus (ijma') and is a cornerstone of responsible ifta. We will examine the definition of raajih and marjuh, the evidence from Ibn Abidin and other scholars, and the limited exceptions where a weak opinion may be followed.

Guided Path
1
Identify the core rule
Read the first sentence of the passage: the mufti must give fatwa on the raajih opinion. This is the rule. Note the terms 'raajih' and 'marjuh' and their opposites.
The rule is stated as an absolute obligation, with exceptions only in specific cases.
2
Analyze the authority cited
The text quotes Ibn Abidin al-Shami from Sharh Uqud Rasm al-Mufti. Understand that Ibn Abidin is a later authoritative Hanafi jurist. His statement makes the rule binding on both the mufti and the layperson who acts.
Ibn Abidin is considered a muhaqqiq (verifier) of the madhhab.
3
Examine the supporting evidence
The passage mentions that Ibn Abidin cited six scholars. The first is Ibn Hajar al-Makki, quoting Imam Nawawi. Note that the principle is supported by consensus (ijma') as indicated by 'there is no disagreement.'
The consensus argument strengthens the obligation.
4
Define the exception
The text says 'except for a few places' (siva'e chand maqamat) where acting on a weak opinion is permissible. Identify that this is a limited exception, not a general license.
These exceptions will be detailed later in the poem (nazm).
5
Apply the principle to a scenario
Consider a practical case: two Hanafi opinions on a transaction—one raajih (permissible), one marjuh (disliked). The mufti must issue the fatwa based on the raajih opinion. If he chooses the marjuh without necessity, his fatwa is invalid.
This highlights the thamarah al-khilaf (practical consequence) of violating the rule.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
پہلا اصول مفتی کا راجح قول پر فتوی دینا فقہ حنفی کے بے شمار مسائل ایسے ہیں جن کی بابت فقہاء احناف سے مختلف اور متعارض اقوال مروی ہیں، جن میں سے بعض کو اصحاب الترجیح نے راجح اور مفتی بہ جبکہ بعض کو مرجوح اور ضعیف قرار دیا ہے، ان مسائل کی بابت فتوی دینے کے سلسلے میں بنیادی اصول یہ ہے کہ فتوی ہمیشہ اس قول پر دیں گے جس کو اصحاب الترجیح نے راجح قرار دیا ہو، مرجوح اور ضعیف قول پر عمل کرنا یا فتوی دینا اجماع کے خلاف ہے، علامہ ابن عابدین شامی فرماتے ہیں: اس شخص پر جو خود عمل کرنا چاہتا ہو یا دوسروں کو فتوی دینا چاہتا ہو لازم ہے کہ وہ اس قول کی پیروی کرے جس کو اس کے مذہب کے علماء نے راجح قرار دیا ہے، پس اس کے لیے (عام حالات میں) مرجوح قول پر عمل کرنا یا فتوی دینا جائز نہیں ہے، سوائے چند مقامات کے کہ ان میں ضعیف قول پر عمل کرنے اور فتوی دینے کی گنجائش ہے جیسا کہ نظم میں (ان مقامات کا تذکرہ) آئے گا۔ (شرح عقود رسم المفتی: ۴۳) علامہ ابن عابدین شامی نے اس اصول کی تائید میں چھ فقہاء کرام کے اقوال نقل کئے ہیں جو بالترتیب درج ذیل ہیں: ۱۔ محقق ابن حجر مکی کی "الفتاوی الکبری" میں ہے: امام نووی نے زوائد الروضہ میں لکھا ہے کہ مفتی اور عمل کرنے والے کے لیے جائز نہیں ہے کہ وہ بغیر غور وفکر کئے ہوئے دو قولوں یا دو وجہوں میں سے جس پر چاہے فتوی دے یا عمل کرے اور اس سلسلہ میں کسی کا اختلاف نہیں ہے، اور زوائد الروضہ کے مصنف سے پہلے علامہ ابن الصلاح نے مفتی اور عمل کرنے والے دونوں کے سلسلہ میں اور علامہ باجی مالکی نے صرف مفتی کے سلسلے میں (راجح قول پر فتوی کے واجب ہونے اور مرجوح
First Principle: The Mufti Giving Fatwa Upon the Rajih Opinion. In Hanafi fiqh, there are countless issues regarding which conflicting and differing statements are narrated from Hanafi jurists, among which some have been declared raajih (preponderant) and mufta bih (to be acted upon) by the people of tarjeeh (preference), while others have been declared marjuh (weak) and da'if. The basic principle regarding giving fatwa on these issues is that the fatwa will always be given on the opinion that the people of tarjeeh have declared raajih. Acting upon or giving fatwa upon a marjuh (weak) opinion is against consensus. Allamah Ibn Abidin al-Shami says: It is necessary for the one who wants to act himself or give fatwa to others to follow the opinion that the scholars of his madhhab have declared raajih. Therefore, it is not permissible for him (in normal circumstances) to act upon or give fatwa on a marjuh opinion, except for a few places where there is scope to act upon and give fatwa on a weak opinion, as will come in the poem. (Sharh Uqud Rasm al-Mufti, p. 43) Allamah Ibn Abidin al-Shami quoted the statements of six great jurists in support of this principle, which are as follows: 1. In al-Fatawa al-Kubra of Muhaqqiq Ibn Hajar al-Makki: Imam Nawawi wrote in Zawa'id al-Rawdah that it is not permissible for a mufti and a person acting (upon a ruling) to give fatwa or act upon whichever of two opinions or two reasons they wish without careful consideration, and there is no disagreement in this regard. Before the author of Zawa'id al-Rawdah, Allamah Ibn al-Salah (said the same) regarding both the mufti and the acting person, and Allamah al-Baji al-Maliki (said the same) regarding only the mufti (about the obligation of giving fatwa on the raajih opinion and the prohibition of the marjuh).
Source pages p. 46

Commentary
This passage states the first and fundamental principle of fatwa: the mufti must always issue fatwas according to the raajih (preponderant) opinion as determined by the scholars of tarjeeh in the Hanafi madhhab. Acting on or issuing a fatwa based on a marjuh (weak) opinion is generally impermissible and contrary to scholarly consensus (ijma'). The author then quotes Allamah Ibn Abidin from Sharh Uqud Rasm al-Mufti, who emphasizes this rule and indicates that exceptions exist only in certain places, which will be discussed later. To further support the principle, Ibn Abidin cited the statements of six great jurists. The passage provides the first of these six references: from Ibn Hajar al-Makki's al-Fatawa al-Kubra, quoting Imam Nawawi from Zawa'id al-Rawdah, stating that it is not permissible for a mufti or a layperson to arbitrarily pick between two conflicting opinions without proper consideration. This principle is a cornerstone of Hanafi ifta methodology, ensuring consistency and preventing arbitrary selection of opinions.

Key Terms
راجح (raajih): The preponderant or stronger opinion in the madhhab, as identified by the scholars of tarjeeh.
مرجوح (marjuh): The weak or outweighed opinion, which is not to be acted upon or given as fatwa in normal circumstances.
اصحاب الترجیح (ashab al-tarjeeh): The scholars who are qualified to weigh and determine the preponderant opinions within the madhhab.
مفتی بہ (mufta bihi): The opinion upon which fatwa is given; the operative ruling in the madhhab.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the first principle of fatwa in the Hanafi madhhab, what is the general ruling on issuing a fatwa based on a marjuh (weak) opinion?
A) It is permissible if the mufti finds it easier.
B) It is impermissible in normal circumstances, except for a few special cases.
C) It is always permissible as long as there is some scholarly support.
D) It is the preferred practice to avoid conflict.

Key Takeaway
A mufti is obligated to issue fatwas according to the raajih (preponderant) opinion; following the marjuh (weak) opinion in normal circumstances is against consensus and invalid.

Rasm al-Mufti — Rules of Issuing Fatwa
intermediate
10 min
First Principle: Mufti Must Follow the Rājiḥ Opinion
Establishes the binding rule with ijma' proofs

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify when a mufti must use the rājiḥ opinion and apply the prohibition of giving fatwa on the marjūḥ opinion.

Core Idea
A mufti must only give fatwa on the rājiḥ opinion; the marjūḥ is forbidden by consensus.

This lesson establishes the foundational rule that governs every fatwa in the Hanafi madhhab. The mufti does not have the freedom to choose any opinion he likes; he is bound by the rājiḥ as determined by the scholars of tarjīḥ. The passage cites several authorities confirming this principle with consensus.

Guided Path
1
Identify the rule
State the principle: fatwa must be on the rājiḥ opinion. Recognize the prohibition: marjūḥ is against consensus.
The rule applies to both the mufti and the layperson acting on his own.
2
Examine the evidence
Review the quotations from Ibn 'Ābidīn, Ibn Ḥajar al-Makkī, al-Qarāfī, Qāsim ibn Quṭlūghbughā, and Ibn Farḥūn. Note that they all support the same rule.
Consensus (ijma') is cited as the basis.
3
Note the exception
The text mentions that in a few specific cases, acting on or giving fatwa on the marjūḥ is permitted. These will be discussed later.
The exception does not negate the general rule.
4
Understand the scope
The rule applies to all muftis, regardless of their level (mujtahid or muqallid), except where the mujtahid faces genuinely conflicting evidences and cannot perform tarjīḥ.
Al-Qarāfī clarifies this condition.

(شرح عقود رسم المفتى : ٤٥)
First principle: The mufti's giving fatwa on the rājiḥ opinion. The treatise 'Uqūd Rasm al-Muftī: There are countless issues in Hanafi fiqh regarding which different and conflicting opinions are narrated from Hanafi jurists, some of which the people of tarjīḥ have deemed rājiḥ and muftā bih, while others they have deemed marjūḥ and weak. The basic principle regarding giving fatwa on these issues is that fatwa will always be given on the opinion that the people of tarjīḥ have considered rājiḥ. Acting upon or giving fatwa on a marjūḥ and weak opinion is against consensus. 'Allāmah Ibn 'Ābidīn al-Shāmī says: It is binding on a person who wants to act himself or give fatwa to others to follow the opinion that the scholars of his madhhab have considered rājiḥ. Therefore, for him (in general circumstances) it is not permissible to act upon or give fatwa on the marjūḥ opinion, except for a few places in which there is room to act upon and give fatwa on the weak opinion – as will be mentioned in the poem. (Sharḥ 'Uqūd Rasm al-Muftī: 43)

'Allāmah Ibn 'Ābidīn al-Shāmī quoted the statements of six noble jurists in support of this principle, which are as follows:

1. In Muḥaqqiq Ibn Ḥajar al-Makkī's al-Fatāwā al-Kubrā: Imam Nawawī wrote in Zawā'id al-Rawḍah that it is not permissible for a mufti or one who acts to give fatwa or act upon whichever of two opinions or two reasons he wishes without deliberation and reflection, and there is no disagreement about this. And before the author of Zawā'id al-Rawḍah, 'Allāmah Ibn al-Ṣalāḥ transmitted consensus regarding both the mufti and the one who acts, and 'Allāmah al-Bājī al-Mālikī transmitted consensus regarding only the mufti (concerning the obligation of giving fatwa on the rājiḥ opinion and the impermissibility of giving fatwa on the marjūḥ opinion). (Sharḥ 'Uqūd Rasm al-Muftī: 44)

2. The words of Imam Abū al-'Abbās Shihāb al-Dīn Aḥmad ibn Idrīs al-Qarāfī also indicate that it is not permissible for both the mujtahid and the muqallid to judge or give fatwa on the marjūḥ opinion, because that is following one's desire, which is forbidden by consensus. This prohibition applies to the mujtahid fī al-madhhab when the evidences are not conflicting in his view and he is not incapable of tarjīḥ. Because for the mujtahid muqallid, at that time (i.e., when evidences conflict and he is incapable of tarjīḥ), it is permissible by consensus to judge on either of the two opinions. (Sharḥ 'Uqūd Rasm al-Muftī: 44)

3. 'Allāmah Qāsim ibn Quṭlūghbughā said at the beginning of his book al-Tarjīḥ wa al-Taṣḥīḥ li al-Qudūrī: I know about a person who follows his desires regarding the madhhab of our imams, to the extent that I heard some judges say: What is the harm in that? So I said: Yes! There is harm in giving fatwa on the weak opinion, because following one's carnal desire is forbidden, and the marjūḥ opinion compared to the rājiḥ opinion is like a non-existent thing. (Sharḥ 'Uqūd Rasm al-Muftī: 45)

4. In 'Allāmah Burhān al-Dīn Ibrāhīm ibn 'Alī ibn Farḥūn al-Ya'murī's Tabṣirat al-Ḥukkām fī Uṣūl al-Aqḍiyah wa Manāhij al-Aḥkām: A person who is not aware of the well-known opinion/transmission among two versions or two opinions, it is not permissible for him to follow his desire and decide on whichever of the two he wishes without deliberating in tarjīḥ. (Sharḥ 'Uqūd Rasm al-Muftī: 45)
Source pages p. 46

Commentary
This passage introduces the first and foundational principle of iftā' in the Hanafi madhhab: the mufti must always issue fatwa based on the rājiḥ (preponderant) opinion as determined by the scholars of tarjīḥ. The author begins by stating that Hanafi fiqh contains numerous issues with multiple and conflicting opinions. The basic rule is that fatwa is given on the opinion considered rājiḥ by the authorities on tarjīḥ. Acting on or giving fatwa on a marjūḥ (weak) opinion is contrary to ijma' (consensus). The author then quotes Ibn 'Ābidīn al-Shāmī from Sharḥ 'Uqūd Rasm al-Muftī who makes it obligatory for both the person acting and the mufti to follow the rājiḥ opinion; deviation is impermissible except in certain exceptional cases that will be mentioned later. To reinforce this principle, Ibn 'Ābidīn provides six testimonies from earlier scholars. The first is from Ibn Ḥajar al-Makkī's al-Fatāwā al-Kubrā, who quotes Imam Nawawī stating it is not permissible for a mufti or a layman to randomly choose between two opinions without investigation; there is consensus on this. Ibn al-Ṣalāḥ and al-Bājī (Maliki) also transmitted consensus. The second testimony is from the Maliki jurist al-Qarāfī, who says neither a mujtahid nor a muqallid may judge or give fatwa on a marjūḥ opinion because it amounts to following desires, which is unanimously forbidden. However, a mujtahid fī al-madhhab may choose either opinion if the evidences are truly conflicting and he is incapable of tarjīḥ. The third is from Qāsim ibn Quṭlūghbughā, who strongly rebukes those who follow whims in madhhab matters, equating the marjūḥ to a non-existent thing. The fourth (and last quoted here) is from Ibn Farḥūn, who says that if a judge or mufti does not know which opinion is the well-known (mashhūr), he may not decide without proper deliberation. These quotations collectively establish the gravity of adhering to the rājiḥ and avoiding the marjūḥ in fatwa.

Key Terms
راجح (rājiḥ): The preponderant or stronger opinion among conflicting views, as determined by the scholars of tarjīḥ based on evidence and methodology.
مرجوح (marjūḥ): The weaker opinion that is outweighed by the rājiḥ; considered like a non-existent ruling for practical purposes.
مفتی بہ (muftā bihī): The opinion upon which fatwa is given; synonymous with the rājiḥ in this context.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the first principle of rasm al-mufti, what is the ruling on giving fatwa based on a marjūḥ (weak) opinion in normal circumstances?
A) It is permissible if the mufti finds it easier.
B) It is impermissible because it goes against consensus and follows personal desire.
C) It is allowed only if the mufti is a mujtahid.
D) It is recommended as a way to preserve the weaker opinion.

Key Takeaway
The first principle of Hanafi iftā: fatwa must be based on the rājiḥ opinion; the marjūḥ is not to be used except in specific exceptional cases.

Rasm al-Mufti — Rules of Issuing Fatwa

The Obligation of Following the Rajih Opinion
Quoting Ibn al-Salah and al-Baji on the necessity of tarjih

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the obligation of tarjih in ifta and apply it to evaluate mufti conduct.

Core Idea
The mufti must only issue fatwa based on the rajih opinion; arbitrary selection is forbidden by consensus.

This passage from Naseem al-Mufti establishes a foundational rule of mufti conduct: tarjih is obligatory. It quotes two major scholars—Ibn al-Salah and al-Baji—to emphasize that a mufti who acts without tarjih is ignorant and acting against consensus. The lesson will unpack the rule, its basis in ijma, and its practical application.

Guided Path
1
Identify the rule
Recognize that the mufti must base his fatwa on the preponderant (rajih) opinion, not just any valid opinion.
This is a core principle of rasm al-mufti.
2
Understand the prohibition
Ibn al-Salah explicitly states that acting without tarjih is ignorance and opposition to consensus.
Ijma is a primary source, so this is a weighty prohibition.
3
Learn from the incident
Al-Baji’s story shows that even jurists erred when they gave a fatwa based on a weak opinion to please a powerful person, later admitting ignorance.
The mufti's loyalty is to the shari'ah, not to the questioner.
4
Apply the rule
In any ifta scenario, the mufti must first identify all relevant opinions within his school, then perform tarjih based on established criteria, and issue the fatwa accordingly.
Tarjih methods include evaluating proofs, the majority practice (shuhrah), and later fatwa reliance.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
امام ابو عمر و عثمان ابن الصلاح نے ”آداب المفتی“ میں کہا ہے: جان لو! کہ جو شخص صرف اس بات کو کافی سمجھتا ہو کہ اس کا فتوی یا اس کا عمل مسئلہ میں کسی قول یا وجہ کے موافق ہو جائے اور ترجیح میں غور و فکر کیے بغیر ان اقوال و وجوہ میں سے جس پر چاہتا ہو عمل کرتا ہو تو وہ نادان ہے اور اجماع کے خلاف عمل کرنے والا ہے۔ (شرح عقود رسم المفتى: ٤٦) قاضی ابوالولید سلیمان بن خلف باجی مالکی نے ایک واقعہ نقل کیا ہے کہ ان کے ساتھ ایک معاملہ پیش آیا تو فقہاء نے اس سلسلے میں انھیں ایسا فتوی دیا جس میں ان کا نقصان تھا، پھر جب علامہ باجی نے ان لوگوں سے براہ راست ( سوال کیا تو انھوں نے کہا کہ ہمیں معلوم نھیں تھا کہ وہ آپ کا معاملہ ہے اور انھوں نے دوسری روایت پر فتوی دیا جوان کے مقصد کے مطابق تھا، علامہ باجی فرماتے ہیں: اور اس صورت کے عدم جواز میں ان مسلمانوں کے درمیان کوئی اختلاف نہیں ہے جن کی بات اجماع کے لیے معتبر سمجھی جاتی ہے۔ (شرح عقود رسم المفتى: ٤٦)
Imam Abu 'Amr 'Uthman ibn al-Salah said in 'Adab al-Mufti': Know! That a person who considers it sufficient that his fatwa or his action agrees with any saying or proof in a matter, and without considering preference (tarjih) acts on whichever of those sayings and proofs he wishes, then he is ignorant and acting against consensus. (Sharh 'Uqud Rasm al-Mufti: 46) Qadi Abu al-Walid Sulayman ibn Khalaf al-Baji al-Maliki narrated an incident: a matter came to him, and the jurists gave him a fatwa that was to his detriment. Then when al-Baji directly asked them, they said, 'We did not know it was your matter,' and they gave a fatwa according to another narration that suited his purpose. Al-Baji says: And there is no disagreement among those Muslims whose word is considered for ijma regarding the impermissibility of this situation. (Sharh 'Uqud Rasm al-Mufti: 46)
Source pages p. 48

Commentary
This passage presents a fundamental rule of ifta: the mufti must issue fatwa based on the preponderant (rajih) opinion, not any opinion. Ibn al-Salah states that acting without tarjih is ignorance and contrary to ijma. Al-Baji's incident illustrates that even if a fatwa agrees with a valid opinion, if it is not the one preferred in that madhhab or context, and the mufti chooses it simply because it suits a person's desire, it is impermissible. This establishes that tarjih is essential; the mufti cannot pick and choose arbitrarily. The rule is based on the principle that fatwa is a form of testimony (shahadah) on behalf of the Shari'ah, requiring accuracy and responsibility.

Key Terms
راجح (rajih): The preponderant opinion among conflicting views, determined by specific tarjih principles.
مرجوح (marjuh): The weaker opinion that is not to be acted upon in fatwa.
اجماع (ijma): Consensus of the scholars of the ummah on the impermissibility of following a marjuh opinion without tarjih.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to Ibn al-Salah, what is the status of a mufti who gives a fatwa based on any opinion without performing tarjih?
A) He is a knowledgeable mufti acting correctly.
B) He is ignorant and acting against ijma.
C) He is permitted because all opinions are valid.
D) He is only blameworthy if the fatwa harms someone.

Key Takeaway
The mufti must always perform tarjih and issue fatwa based on the preponderant opinion; arbitrary selection is prohibited by scholarly consensus.

The Necessity of Tarjih Principles
Introducing the need for tarjih principles among conflicting opinions

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the obligation of the mufti to follow only rajih opinions and explain the necessity of learning objective tarjih principles.

Core Idea
Tarjih is not arbitrary; it relies on objective criteria recorded in classical usul works.

You have learned that a mufti must follow the rajih opinion. But how does one determine which opinion is rajih? This lesson introduces the second principle: the methodology of tarjih. The passage emphasizes that giving fatwa without proper investigation is a grave sin, and that the mufti must learn the rules that distinguish stronger from weaker opinions. We will explore the sources of these rules and the duty to apply them.

Guided Path
1
Identify the obligation
The passage states that the mufti must follow only the rajih opinion; giving fatwa on a marjuh opinion is against ijma. Locate the sentence that makes this clear.
The phrase 'مفتی کے لئے صرف راجح قول کی پیروی کرنا واجب ہے' is key.
2
Recognize the need for principles
The text says it is necessary for the mufti to become acquainted with the rules and principles that determine which opinion is rajih. Why is this necessary? Because without objective criteria, tarjih would be subjective.
Note the phrase 'اصول وضوابط سے واقف ہونا بھی ضروری ہے'.
3
Understand the gravity
Quoting Fatawa Khayriyya, the passage warns that muftis and qadis must give investigated (researched) answers, not estimated ones. They must fear Allah and avoid following personal desires. Identify the consequences of neglecting this duty.
The phrase 'حلال کو حرام کر کے یا حرام کو حلال کر کے اللہ تعالی پر افتراء' shows the severity.
4
Locate the sources
The passage references two key sources: Allamah Shami's Sharh 'Uqud Rasm al-Mufti and Mufti Khalid Saifullah Rahmani's al-Fatawa. These works record the rules for tarjih. Why are these authoritative?
Sharh 'Uqud Rasm al-Mufti is a foundational text in Hanafi iftaa methodology.
5
Apply the principle
The mufti must use these rules to determine the rajih opinion and then issue fatwa accordingly. The passage implies that tarjih is a skill that must be learned. Reflect on how a mufti would apply these rules in practice.
Consider that tarjih involves analyzing textual strength, consistency with usul, and later practice.

The Second Principle: Principles of Preference (Tarjih) among Conflicting Opinions. Sharh 'Uqud Rasm al-Mufti: When you have understood that it is obligatory upon the mufti to follow only the rajih opinion and giving fatwa on the marjuh opinion is against ijma, then it is also necessary for you to become acquainted with those rules and principles on the basis of which one opinion is considered rajih and another marjuh among conflicting opinions. Thus, in Fatawa Khayriyya it is stated: There is no doubt that recognizing the rajih and marjuh opinions among disputed issues and knowing their levels in terms of strength and weakness is the ultimate aspiration of those who strive for knowledge. Therefore, it is obligatory upon the mufti and the qadi to give investigated answers, not estimated answers. They should fear fabricating against Allah by making halal haram or haram halal. Following one's desire and inclining towards that wealth (worldly gain) is haram, which is a great calamity and immense tribulation, because giving fatwa is a delicate matter. Only the ignorant and unfortunate dare in this matter. (Sharh 'Uqud Rasm al-Mufti: 54) In most themes of Allamah Shami's monumental book Sharh 'Uqud Rasm al-Mufti, these rules and regulations are recorded on the basis of which distinction is made between rajih and marjuh opinions and on which the preferences of the mashayikh are reconciled. The faqih of the age, Maulana Khalid Saifullah Rahmani, also briefly indicated in the introduction of his book al-Fatawa the causes on the basis of which an opinion among conflicting opinions is considered rajih, and in case of disagreement in tarjih, one acts on the preference of one faqih. Commentary
This passage introduces the second core principle of rasm al-mufti: the need for a systematic methodology (usul) for tarjih. After establishing that only rajih opinions should be followed, the text explains that the mufti must learn the rules that differentiate rajih from marjuh. It quotes Fatawa Khayriyya emphasizing that recognizing these levels is a high goal; muftis and qadis must give researched answers, not guesses, fearing Allah's displeasure. The passage then references Allamah Shami's Sharh 'Uqud Rasm al-Mufti as the key work expounding these rules, and Mufti Khalid Saifullah Rahmani's al-Fatawa as a modern summary. The main point: tarjih is not arbitrary but based on objective criteria such as the strength of evidence, the reliability of narrators, the practice of the majority of scholars, and later fatwa practice. This principle ensures consistency and prevents personal whims.

Key Terms
ترجیح (tarjih): The process of determining which opinion is preponderant among conflicting views based on established criteria.
متعارض اقوال (muta'arid aqwal): Conflicting opinions within the Hanafi school on the same issue.
راجح (rajih): The stronger opinion after applying tarjih criteria.
مرجوح (marjuh): The weaker opinion that is not acted upon except in necessity.

Key Takeaway
The mufti must learn and apply objective tarjih principles; giving fatwa without proper investigation is a grave sin.

Rasm al-Mufti — Rules of Issuing Fatwa
intermediate
10 min
Principles of Tarjih: The Five Bases for Preference Among Conflicting Hanafi Opinions
Introduction and summary of bases for tarjih

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify and apply the five bases of tarjih within the Hanafi madhhab to determine which opinion is preponderant (rajih) for issuing a fatwa.

Core Idea
A mufti must rely on the rajih opinion using five bases: jurist rank, transmission authority, book type, evidence strength, and preference words.

This lesson introduces the essential principles a mufti must know to choose between conflicting opinions within the Hanafi school. Since issuing fatwa from a marjuh (outweighed) opinion is contrary to scholarly consensus, the mufti must apply these five bases to determine the stronger view. The passage summarizes these bases from the works of later Hanafi masters, warning of the grave responsibility of giving fatwa without proper research.

Guided Path
1
Identify the base of jurist personality (shakhsiyyah)
Recognise that the opinion of a higher-ranking jurist (e.g., a mujtahid fi al-madhhab) is given preference over that of a lower-ranking jurist. This base relies on the hierarchical classification of Hanafi scholars.
The hierarchy of Hanafi jurists (e.g., mujtahidun fi al-shar', mujtahidun fi al-madhhab, etc.) is a key concept in rasm al-mufti.
2
Identify the base of transmission authority (riwayah)
When two conflicting opinions exist, examine which is transmitted through a stronger chain. The well-known opinions from Zahir al-Riwayah (the six core books of Imam Muhammad) are preferred over rare transmissions (Nawadir).
Zahir al-Riwayah is the highest layer; Nawadir are less authoritative.
3
Identify the base of book type (kitab)
Consider whether the opinion appears in a matn (core text) or a sharh (commentary). Matns are given preference because their authors were more selective in transmitting reliable rulings. In case of conflict, the matn view prevails.
Well-known matns include al-Quduri, al-Mukhtar, al-Wiqayah, etc.
4
Identify the base of evidence (dalil)
Determine the underlying evidence: an opinion based on istihsan (juristic preference) generally overrides one based on qiyas (analogy). Istihsan is considered stronger because it relies on a more suitable or equitable reasoning.
In Hanafi usul, istihsan is a recognised source; other schools may not accept it.
5
Identify the base of preference words (alfaz al-tarjih)
Look for technical terms used by jurists to indicate the strength of an opinion. For example, 'asahh' (more correct) is considered weightier than 'asbah' (most fitting). These words are used in fatwa collections to signal the relied-upon view.
Common terms: sahih, asahh, asbah, rajah, mukhtar, etc.

The second principle: Principles of preference among conflicting statements. Sharh Uqud Rasm al-Mufti. When you have come to know that it is obligatory for the mufti to follow only the preponderant (rajih) opinion and that giving fatwa according to the outweighed (marjuh) opinion is contrary to consensus, then it is also necessary for you to become acquainted with those principles and rules on the basis of which one opinion is deemed rajih and the other marjuh. Thus, in Fatawa Khayriyya it is stated: There is no doubt that recognizing the rajih and marjuh opinions among disputed issues and knowing the grades of these opinions in terms of strength and weakness is the ultimate aspiration of those striving for knowledge. Therefore, it is obligatory upon the mufti and judge to give researched answers, not estimated ones, and to fear fabricating lies against Allah by making halal haram or haram halal. Following one's desires or inclining towards wealth (for personal gain) is haram, as it is a great calamity and immense tribulation, because giving fatwa is a delicate matter; only the ignorant and ill-fated ones dare in this regard. (Sharh Uqud Rasm al-Mufti, p. 54) In the majority of the contents of Allamah Shami's invaluable book Sharh Uqud Rasm al-Mufti, those rules and principles are recorded on the basis of which rajih and marjuh opinions are distinguished and on the basis of which reconciliation is made among the preferences of the mashayikh. The jurist of the age, Maulana Khalid Saifullah Rahmani, also briefly indicated in the introduction of Kitab al-Fatawa those causes on the basis of which one opinion among conflicting statements is declared rajih, and in case of difference in preference, the preference of one jurist is acted upon. Maulana states: In those issues where there is difference of opinion among the Hanafi mashayikh, what will be the bases of tarjih? Various causes and reasons have been mentioned in this regard, whose summary is as follows: 1. Sometimes tarjih is given based on personality, for example, the opinion of a jurist belonging to a higher class prevails over that of a lower class jurist. 2. Sometimes tarjih is given based on the authority of transmission and narration, i.e., when two conflicting opinions appear in an issue, the opinion that is narrated with a stronger chain is considered rajih. For example, in case of conflict between Zahir al-Riwayah and Riwayat al-Nawadir, the opinion of Zahir al-Riwayah is rajih. 3. Sometimes tarjih is given based on books, because certain authors take care to transmit reliable narrations of the madhhab while other scholars collect all kinds of opinions. For example, in case of conflict between a matn and a sharh, the opinion of the matn is given preference because the grade of a matn is higher than that of a sharh. 4. Sometimes tarjih is given based on evidence, for example, if an opinion is based on istihsan and another is based on qiyas, then generally the opinion based on istihsan is preferred. Sometimes the basis of tarjih are those preference words which jurists use to indicate the correctness of an opinion, for example, if one jurist declares an opinion as 'sahih' and another jurist declares the opposing opinion as 'asbah', then the preference of the one who used the word 'asahh' is considered valid. Benefited from the introduction of Kitab al-Fatawa (1/260-266). Following, with all the contents of Sharh Uqud Rasm al-Mufti in view, the principles and rules of tarjih are presented in somewhat more detail.
Source pages p. 49

Commentary
This passage, from the second principle of the book, sets out the necessity of tarjih (preference) among conflicting opinions within the Hanafi madhhab. The author first establishes that the mufti is obligated to follow only the rajih (preponderant) opinion and that acting on a marjuh (outweighed) opinion is against scholarly consensus. He warns of the grave responsibility of issuing fatwas, quoting Fatawa Khayriyya that identifying the stronger and weaker opinions is the ultimate goal of a seeker of knowledge. The mufti must avoid personal desire and material inducements. The passage then introduces the five bases of tarjih as summarized by Maulana Khalid Saifullah Rahmani from the introduction of his Kitab al-Fatawa (later detailed from Sharh Uqud Rasm al-Mufti). These bases are: (1) Personal rank of the jurist — higher-ranking jurists' opinions are given weight over lower-ranking ones. (2) Authority of transmission — opinions from Zahir al-Riwayah (the six core books of Imam Muhammad) prevail over those from Nawadir (rare transmissions). (3) Type of book — rulings from matn (core text) are preferred over those from sharh (commentary) because matns are more carefully selected. (4) Legal evidence — opinions based on istihsan (juristic preference) generally override those based on qiyas (analogy) when they conflict. (5) Preference words — technical terms used by scholars such as 'sahih' (correct) or 'asahh' (more correct) indicate the strength of an opinion; for example, 'asahh' is considered weightier than 'asbah' (most fitting). The passage concludes by stating that these principles will be elaborated in detail in the subsequent pages of the commentary.

Key Terms
ظاہر الروایہ (Zahir al-Riwayah): The well-known and transmitted opinions from the six core books of Imam Muhammad al-Shaybani (al-Mabsut, al-Jami' al-Saghir, al-Jami' al-Kabir, al-Siyar al-Saghir, al-Siyar al-Kabir, al-Ziyadat). They are considered the most authoritative layer of Hanafi rulings.
روایۃ النوادر (Riwayat al-Nawadir): Rare or solitary transmissions, often from Hasan ibn Ziyad or other early Hanafi scholars, that are not part of the six core books. They are given lower weight in tarjih.
استحسان (Istihsan): Juristic preference that departs from strict qiyas in favor of a ruling that is more suitable or equitable, based on a stronger evidence or custom. In this text, it is considered stronger than qiyas in tarjih.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, when a conflict occurs between a ruling from Zahir al-Riwayah and a ruling from Riwayat al-Nawadir, which opinion is given preference?
A) The opinion from Riwayat al-Nawadir
B) The opinion from Zahir al-Riwayah
C) The opinion based on istihsan
D) The opinion that is more commonly practiced

Key Takeaway
The mufti must use the five bases of tarjih (personality, transmission, book type, evidence, preference words) to resolve conflicting Hanafi opinions and issue fatwa from the strongest opinion.

Rasm al-Mufti — Rules of Issuing Fatwa
beginner
8 min
First Four Principles of Personality-Based Preference
Introducing the first four rules for preferring Hanafi opinions based on jurist hierarchy

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify and apply the first four rules of personality-based preference (tarjīḥ bi al-shakhṣiyyah) when issuing a fatwa in the Hanafi madhhab.

Core Idea
Mufti must follow a strict hierarchy: single opinion, Imam Abu Hanifa alone, later tarjīḥ scholars, or unanimous three Imams.

In the Hanafi madhhab, not all jurists are equal in rank. When issuing a fatwa, a mufti must navigate between opinions based on the personality and authority of the jurist. This lesson introduces the first four of thirteen principles that govern this process, providing a clear hierarchy for preferring one opinion over another.

Guided Path
1
Identify the first rule
State the principle: when only one opinion is transmitted from the Hanafi school, it must be followed regardless of its source (Zāhir al-Riwāyah, Nawādir, or later fatāwā), unless its underlying cause ('illah) is clearly no longer present.
This establishes the default authority of a single transmitted opinion.
2
Identify the second rule
State the principle: when only Imam Abū Ḥanīfah's opinion is transmitted, it is followed directly without needing endorsement from later scholars of tarjīḥ. His authority is sufficient.
Imam Abū Ḥanīfah's position is self-authenticating within the madhhab.
3
Identify the third rule
State the principle: when multiple opinions are transmitted, the mufti must follow the opinion preferred by the scholars of tarjīḥ (aṣḥāb al-tarjīḥ), regardless of whether they preferred Imam Abū Ḥanīfah or his two companions.
These scholars are experts in evidence and their tarjīḥ is binding.
4
Identify the fourth rule
State the principle: when the three founding Imams (Abū Ḥanīfah, Abū Yūsuf, and Muḥammad) agree on a matter, their consensus is binding and cannot be overridden by later ijtihād.
Later scholars cannot match their access to evidence and insight.

شخصیت کی بنیاد پر ترجیح کے تیرہ (۱۳) اصول
فقہ حنفی کے مختلف فیہ اقوال میں سے کسی قول کو بعض اوقات شخصیت کی بنیاد پر ترجیح دی جاتی ہے، کیوں کہ علمی اور تحقیقی اعتبار سے تمام فقہاء یکساں صفات کے حامل نہیں ہوتے ہیں ، ان میں فقہی بصیرت اور دلائل کی معرفت کے اعتبار سے نمایاں فرق ہوتا ہے، لہذا جو فقیہ علم وتحقیق میں بڑھا ہوا ہو اس کا قول اپنے مقابل کی بہ نسبت زیادہ راجح ہوگا، نیز ایسا بھی ہوتا ہے کہ ایک فقیہ کو بسا اوقات ممارست اور تجربہ کی بنیاد پر فقہ کے مختلف ابواب میں سے کسی مخصوص باب میں زیادہ مہارت ہو جاتی ہے، جب کہ اس باب میں دوسرے فقیہ کو اتنی مہارت نہیں ہوتی ہے، لہذا ایسی صورت میں اس باب کے ماہر مفتی کے قول کو دوسرے فقیہ کے قول پر مقدم کرنالازم ہوگا، اسی طرح کبھی کبھی کسی مسئلہ میں احناف کے ائمہ ثلاثہ کا اتفاق ہونے کے باوجود متاخرین فقہاء کا اس مسئلہ میں اختلاف ہو جاتا ہے، لہذا ان تمام صورتوں میں فتوی دیتے وقت درج ذیل اصولوں کی پابندی ضروری ہوگی:

الرسائل الزينيه فى مذهب الحنفيه :١٢١،١١٥،١٠٧،المذهب الحنفى: ٢٥٠/١)
تیسرا اصول : جب احناف سے مختلف اقوال مروی ہوں
جب کسی مسئلہ میں فقہاء احناف سے مختلف اقوال مروی ہوں تو مفتی اس قول پر فتوی دے گا جس کو علماء مذہب میں سے اصحاب الترجیح نے راجح قرار دیا ہو، خواہ ان حضرات نے امام اعظم کے قول کو ترجیح دی ہو یا صاحبین کے قول کو ، کیوں کہ یہ حضرات امام اعظم اور صاحبین دونوں کے دلائل سے واقف تھے، ان کے بارے میں یہ گمان نہیں کیا جاسکتا کہ انھوں نے امام اعظم کے قول کو چھوڑ کر صاحبین کے قول پر اس لئے فتوی دیا کہ وہ امام صاحب کی دلیل پر مطلع نہیں ہو سکے، کیوں کہ ہم دیکھتے ہیں کہ انھوں نے اپنی کتابوں کو دونوں کے دلائل سے بھر دیا اور وقت ہے اور انھوں نے اپنے آپ کو مذہب حنفی کی تائید و تنقیح کے لئے وقف کر دیا تھا، پس ان کی ترجیح پر عمل کرنا واجب ہوگا، خواہ وہ کسی بھی فقیہ کے قول کو راجح قرار دیں۔
(شرح عقود رسم المفتى: ۱۱۰،۱۰٦،٨٧) ا
چوتھا اصول : جب کسی مسئلہ میں احناف کا اتفاق ہو
جب کسی مسئلہ میں احناف کے ائمہ ثلاثہ یعنی امام ابوحنیفہ، امام ابو یوسف اور امام محمد کا اتفاق ہو تو انھیں کے قول پر عمل کرنا اور فتوی دینا واجب ہے،خواہ مفتی مجہتد ہو یا غیر مجتہد، کیوں کہ بعد کے کسی بھی مجتہد کا اجتہاد ان ائمہ کے اجتہاد سے قوی نہیں ہوسکتا اور نہ ہی بعد کے کسی مجتہد کی نظر متقدمین ائمہ کی نظر سے باریک ہوسکتی ہے، لہذا ظاہر بات یہ ہے کہ انھوں نے اپنے قول کی بنیاد کسی ایسی دلیل پر رکھی ہوگی جہاں تک بعد والے مجتہد مفتی کی رسائی نہیں ہوسکی، پس ائمہ ثلاثہ کے قول سے عدول کرنا جائز نہیں ہوگا۔
شرح عقود رسم المفتى: ٩٩،٩٥)
The Mufti's Name in the Solution of the Principles of Sharh Uqud Rasm al-Mufti. The thirteen (13) principles of preference based on personality. Sometimes preference is given to one opinion among the different opinions of the Hanafi school based on personality, because not all jurists possess equal qualities in knowledge and research; there is a clear difference between them in fiqh insight and knowledge of evidence. Therefore, the opinion of a jurist who is advanced in knowledge and research will be more preponderant compared to his counterpart. Also, sometimes a jurist develops greater expertise in a particular chapter of fiqh due to practice and experience, while another jurist does not have that expertise in that chapter. Hence, in such a case, it is necessary to give precedence to the opinion of the expert mufti of that chapter over the opinion of the other jurist. Similarly, sometimes there is disagreement among later jurists about a matter despite the agreement of the three Imams (Abu Hanifa, Abu Yusuf, and Muhammad). Therefore, when issuing a fatwa in all these situations, it is necessary to adhere to the following principles: First principle: When only one narration is transmitted from the Hanafis. When only one opinion is transmitted from the Hanafi jurists regarding a matter, it is obligatory to act upon that opinion and issue a fatwa according to it, whether the matter is taken from Zahir al-Riwayah, Nawadir, or Fatawa and Waqai'. But if it becomes evidently known that the answer to this matter was based on a cause (illah) that no longer exists, then acting upon that opinion is not obligatory. (Usul al-Ifta wa Adabuhu: 198, al-Madkhal al-Mufassal ila al-Fiqh al-Hanafi li Abi al-Hajj: 523) Second principle: Issuing fatwa according to the opinion of Imam A'zam. When only the opinion of Imam Abu Hanifa is transmitted regarding a matter, we will issue a fatwa according to it, whether any jurist among the scholars of the school explicitly declared the correction and preference of that opinion or not. Allamah Ibn Bujaym says: The opinion of Imam Abu Hanifa does not need the preference and correction of the scholars of the school. Therefore, when his opinion is found regarding a matter, it will be acted upon, whether the scholars explicitly declared its authenticity or not. (al-Rasa'il al-Zayniyya fi Madhhab al-Hanafiyya: 121,115,107, al-Madhhab al-Hanafi: 250/1) Third principle: When different opinions are transmitted from the Hanafis. When different opinions are transmitted from the Hanafi jurists regarding a matter, the mufti will issue a fatwa according to the opinion that the scholars of preference among the scholars of the school have declared preponderant, whether those scholars gave preference to the opinion of Imam A'zam or to the opinion of the two Companions (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad). Because these scholars were aware of the evidence of both Imam A'zam and the two Companions. It cannot be assumed about them that they abandoned the opinion of Imam A'zam and issued a fatwa according to the opinion of the two Companions because they could not access the Imam's evidence. We see that they filled their books with the evidence of both and they devoted themselves to supporting and refining the Hanafi school. Therefore, it is obligatory to act upon their preference, regardless of which jurist's opinion they declared preponderant. (Sharh Uqud Rasm al-Mufti: 110,106,87) Fourth principle: When the Hanafis agree. When the three Imams of the Hanafis—Imam Abu Hanifa, Imam Abu Yusuf, and Imam Muhammad—agree on a matter, it is obligatory to act upon their opinion and issue a fatwa according to it, whether the mufti is a mujtahid or non-mujtahid. Because the ijtihad of any later mujtahid cannot be stronger than the ijtihad of these Imams, nor can the view of a later mujtahid be more subtle than the view of the early Imams. Therefore, it is obvious that they based their opinion on some evidence that later mujtahid muftis could not reach. Thus, it is not permissible to deviate from the opinion of the three Imams. (Sharh Uqud Rasm al-Mufti: 99,95)
Source pages p. 51

Commentary
This passage is a presentation of the first four rules (from a set of thirteen) for preferring one Hanafi opinion over another based on the personality and rank of the jurist. The author explains that not all Hanafi jurists are equal in knowledge, insight, and familiarity with evidence. Therefore, when a mufti faces multiple opinions, he must follow a hierarchy. The first rule states that if only one opinion is transmitted from the Hanafi school—whether from the core texts (Zahir al-Riwayah), rare transmissions (Nawadir), or later fatwa collections—it must be followed, unless the underlying cause ('illah) has clearly ceased to exist. The second rule governs cases where only Imam Abu Hanifa's opinion is reported: his opinion is authoritative without needing explicit endorsement from later scholars. The third rule addresses situations where multiple opinions are transmitted: the mufti must follow the opinion declared preponderant by the scholars of tarjih (preference) within the school, regardless of whether they preferred the Imam or the two Companions. The fourth rule covers unanimous agreement of the three founding Imams (Abu Hanifa, Abu Yusuf, Muhammad): their consensus is binding and cannot be overridden by later ijtihad, as later mujtahids cannot match their access to evidence. These principles ensure consistency and authority within the Hanafi madhhab.

Key Terms
ظاہر الروایہ (Zāhir al-Riwāyah): The core transmitted rulings from Imam Muhammad's six books, considered the most authoritative layer of the madhhab
نوادر (Nawādir): Rare transmissions not included in Zahir al-Riwayah, such as those of Hasan ibn Ziyad
اصحاب الترجیح (Aṣḥāb al-Tarjīḥ): Scholars within the madhhab who are qualified to prefer one opinion over another based on evidence

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what should a mufti do when only the opinion of Imam Abu Hanifa is transmitted for a specific issue, and no later scholar has explicitly preferred it?
A) He must search for a preferred opinion from later scholars before following it.
B) He must follow it directly without needing any endorsement.
C) He must set it aside and use qiyas to derive a new ruling.
D) He must consult the public to see if they accept it.

Key Takeaway
When faced with multiple Hanafi opinions, the mufti must apply a fixed hierarchy: single opinion, Imam Abu Hanifa alone, later tarjih scholars, or unanimous three Imams – each with its own conditions.

Third Principle: Following the Tarjih of Ashab al-Tarjih
Third principle of Rasm al-Mufti on handling multiple Hanafi opinions

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the third principle of Rasm al-Mufti to identify which Hanafi opinion to follow when multiple opinions exist, and explain the authoritative role of Ashab al-Tarjih.

Core Idea
In Hanafi fatwa, the mufti follows the opinion chosen by Ashab al-Tarjih, not his own preference.

When multiple opinions exist from Hanafi jurists—Imam Abu Hanifa, Abu Yusuf, or Muhammad al-Shaybani—how does a mufti decide which to give fatwa upon? The Hanafi tradition developed a clear hierarchy: the mufti does not weigh evidences himself but relies on the tarjih (weighing) performed by later mujtahids known as Ashab al-Tarjih. This principle ensures consistency and respects the cumulative scholarly effort within the madhhab.

Guided Path
1
Recognize the scenario
The mufti is faced with a legal issue for which there are multiple narrations from Hanafi imams—Imam Abu Hanifa, Abu Yusuf, and Muhammad al-Shaybani (the Sahibayn)—or later authorities within the school.
This is a common occurrence in Hanafi fiqh.
2
Identify the role of Ashab al-Tarjih
Ashab al-Tarjih are specialized scholars who have studied the evidences of all the Hanafi imams. They have determined which opinion is preponderant (rajih) and recorded their preferences in authoritative works.
Examples include al-Karkhi, al-Sarakhsi, and others.
3
Apply the tarjih
The mufti must follow the opinion that Ashab al-Tarjih have deemed preponderant, regardless of whether it is the view of Imam Abu Hanifa or the Sahibayn. This is obligatory (wajib) because of the authority and knowledge of these scholars.
The mufti does not re-do tarjih; he follows the established hierarchy.

Source Text

تیسرا اصول : جب احناف سے مختلف اقوال مروی ہوں
جب کسی مسئلہ میں فقہاء احناف سے مختلف اقوال مروی ہوں تو مفتی اس قول پر فتوی دے گا جس کو علماء مذہب میں سے اصحاب الترجیح نے راجح قرار دیا ہو، خواہ ان حضرات نے امام اعظم کے قول کو ترجیح دی ہو یا صاحبین کے قول کو ، کیوں کہ یہ حضرات امام اعظم اور صاحبین دونوں کے دلائل سے واقف تھے، ان کے بارے میں یہ گمان نہیں کیا جاسکتا کہ انھوں نے امام اعظم کے قول کو چھوڑ کر صاحبین کے قول پر اس لئے فتوی دیا کہ وہ امام صاحب کی دلیل پر مطلع نہیں ہو سکے، کیوں کہ ہم دیکھتے ہیں کہ انھوں نے اپنی کتابوں کو دونوں کے دلائل سے بھر دیا اور وقت ہے اور انھوں نے اپنے آپ کو مذہب حنفی کی تائید و تنقیح کے لئے وقف کر دیا تھا، پس ان کی ترجیح پر عمل کرنا واجب ہوگا، خواہ وہ کسی بھی فقیہ کے قول کو راجح قرار دیں۔
(شرح عقود رسم المفتى: ۱۱۰،۱۰٦،٨٧) ا
Third Principle: When different opinions are narrated from the Hanafis
When in a matter different opinions are narrated from Hanafi jurists, the mufti will give fatwa on the opinion that the scholars of tarjih among the scholars of the madhhab have deemed preponderant, whether those individuals gave preference to the saying of Imam A'zam or to the saying of the two companions (Sahibayn), because these individuals were aware of the evidences of both Imam A'zam and the two companions. It cannot be assumed about them that they left the saying of Imam A'zam and gave fatwa on the saying of the two companions because they could not become informed of Imam's evidence, for we see that they filled their books with the evidences of both and they devoted themselves to supporting and refining the Hanafi madhhab. So acting upon their tarjih is obligatory, whether they deem the saying of any jurist as preponderant.
(Sharh Uqud Rasm al-Mufti: 110, 106, 87)
Source pages p. 52

Commentary
This passage presents the third principle for a mufti in the Hanafi madhhab. It addresses the scenario where multiple opinions exist from Hanafi jurists. The ruling is clear: the mufti must follow the opinion that the scholars of tarjih (those specialized in weighing evidences) have deemed preponderant. The author justifies this by noting that these scholars were fully aware of the evidences of both Imam Abu Hanifa and his two major students (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad al-Shaybani). It would be incorrect to think they preferred the Sahibayn out of ignorance of Imam Abu Hanifa's evidence. Rather, they had thorough knowledge and deliberately chose the stronger position. Therefore, their tarjih is binding, regardless of which imam they favored. This principle ensures consistency and reliance on expert juristic discretion within the madhhab.

Key Terms
اصحاب الترجیح (Ashab al-Tarjih): Scholars within the Hanafi madhhab who specialize in weighing evidences and determining which opinion is preponderant (rajih).
ترجیح (Tarjih): The process of determining which legal opinion is stronger based on evidence, used when multiple opinions exist.

Key Terms from Source Passage

B) The opinion preferred by the scholars of tarjih
C) The opinion of the majority of Hanafi jurists
D) The most recent opinion

Key Takeaway
The mufti must follow the tarjih of Ashab al-Tarjih, as their knowledge of all evidences makes their preference authoritative and binding.

Fourth Principle: Agreement of the Three Imams is Binding
Binding nature of unanimous opinion of the three founding imams

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the principle that agreement of the three imams (Abu Hanifa, Abu Yusuf, Muhammad) is binding on all muftis, regardless of their level of ijtihad.

Core Idea
When the three imams agree, no later ijtihad can override — their ruling is binding on all.

This lesson introduces a foundational principle of Hanafi fatwa hierarchy: when Imam Abu Hanifa, Imam Abu Yusuf, and Imam Muhammad al-Shaybani are unanimous on a ruling, that ruling becomes absolutely binding. This principle ensures the stability of the madhhab and protects the authority of the founding texts (Zahir al-Riwayah). As a mufti, you must know when a ruling is entrenched and cannot be departed from.

Guided Path
1
Identify the trigger
Recognize when the ruling is agreed upon by all three imams: Abu Hanifa, Abu Yusuf, and Muhammad.
This agreement is the strongest form of madhhab position.
2
Recognize the obligation
Understand that acting upon their saying and giving fatwa is wajib (obligatory).
The text uses 'واجب' — it is not merely recommended.
3
Understand the scope
Note that this obligation applies to all muftis, whether mujtahid or non-mujtahid.
Even a mujtahid mufti cannot depart from their consensus.
4
Grasp the reasoning
The ijtihad of later scholars cannot be stronger or more precise than that of the founding imams, who had direct access to evidence.
Later scholars’ rulings are considered weaker in authority.
5
Apply the consequence
Conclude that departing from their consensus is not permissible; the mufti must follow their ruling.
This prevents disruption of the madhhab’s core positions.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
چوتھا اصول : جب کسی مسئلہ میں احناف کا اتفاق ہو
جب کسی مسئلہ میں احناف کے ائمہ ثلاثہ یعنی امام ابوحنیفہ، امام ابو یوسف اور امام محمد کا اتفاق ہو تو انھیں کے قول پر عمل کرنا اور فتوی دینا واجب ہے،خواہ مفتی مجہتد ہو یا غیر مجتہد، کیوں کہ بعد کے کسی بھی مجتہد کا اجتہاد ان ائمہ کے اجتہاد سے قوی نہیں ہوسکتا اور نہ ہی بعد کے کسی مجتہد کی نظر متقدمین ائمہ کی نظر سے باریک ہوسکتی ہے، لہذا ظاہر بات یہ ہے کہ انھوں نے اپنے قول کی بنیاد کسی ایسی دلیل پر رکھی ہوگی جہاں تک بعد والے مجتہد مفتی کی رسائی نہیں ہوسکی، پس ائمہ ثلاثہ کے قول سے عدول کرنا جائز نہیں ہوگا۔
شرح عقود رسم المفتى: ٩٩،٩٥)
Fourth Principle: When there is agreement of the Hanafis in a matter
When in a matter there is agreement of the three imams of the Hanafis, i.e., Imam Abu Hanifa, Imam Abu Yusuf and Imam Muhammad, then acting upon their saying and giving fatwa is obligatory, whether the mufti is a mujtahid or non-mujtahid, because the ijtihad of any later mujtahid cannot be stronger than the ijtihad of these imams, nor can the view of any later mujtahid be more precise than the view of the early imams. Therefore, it is apparent that they based their saying on some evidence to which later mujtahid muftis could not have access. So departing from the saying of the three imams is not permissible.
(Sharh Uqud Rasm al-Mufti: 99, 95)
Source pages p. 52

Commentary
This passage explains the fourth principle: when Imam Abu Hanifa, Imam Abu Yusuf, and Imam Muhammad are all agreed on a ruling, that ruling is absolutely binding on all muftis, whether they are themselves mujtahids or not. The reasoning is that the ijtihad of these founding imams is inherently stronger and more precise than any later ijtihad. They had direct access to the sources and a deeper understanding. Therefore, it is not permissible to deviate from their consensus. This principle safeguards the authority of the founding texts of the madhhab (Zahir al-Riwayah) and prevents later scholars from overriding them.

Key Terms
ائمہ ثلاثہ (A'immah Thalathah): The three founding imams of the Hanafi madhhab: Abu Hanifa, Abu Yusuf, and Muhammad al-Shaybani.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What is the ruling when the three imams (Abu Hanifa, Abu Yusuf, Muhammad) agree on a matter?
A) The mufti may choose any opinion
B) It is obligatory to follow their opinion and give fatwa accordingly
C) Only mujtahid muftis must follow it
D) It is recommended but not obligatory

Key Takeaway
The unanimous opinion of the three founding imams (Abu Hanifa, Abu Yusuf, Muhammad) is absolutely binding on all muftis, and no later ijtihad can override it.

Sixth Principle: When Disagreement Depends on Changing Times and Customs
Principle on tarjih when custom changes

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the sixth principle to identify whether the later Hanafi opinion should be preferred over Imam Abu Hanifa's when the disagreement is based on changing custom.

Core Idea
When custom changes, the later fatwa matching the new custom is preponderant.

This lesson introduces a crucial tarjih principle in Hanafi ifta: the effect of changing custom on the weight of opinions. When Imam Abu Hanifa ruled based on the customs of his time, and later his companions ruled differently because those customs changed, the companions' opinion becomes preponderant. This principle reflects the Hanafi school's recognition that 'urf is a dynamic source of law, and muftis must consider contemporary realities.

Guided Path
1
Identify the nature of the disagreement
Determine whether the difference between Imam Abu Hanifa and the Sahibayn is rooted in a specific custom or circumstance that may change over time.
Not all disagreements are custom-based; some are purely juristic.
2
Assess the custom at each time
Compare the custom ('urf) in Imam Abu Hanifa's era with that in the era of Abu Yusuf and Muhammad. If they differ, the change is relevant.
The custom must be a legitimate, non-contradictory practice.
3
Apply the principle of taghayyur al-ahkam
The ruling changes with the change of custom ('adah muhakkamah), so the opinion that aligns with the current custom is preponderant.
This is a well-known Hanafi legal maxim.
4
Determine the preponderant view
If the above conditions are met, the opinion of the Sahibayn (or later companions) is preponderant (rajih) and should be followed in fatwa.
The later opinion is mufta bihi in such cases.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
چھٹا اصول: امام اعظم اور صاحبین کا اختلاف عصر و زمان پر مبنی ہو
جب کسی مسئلہ میں امام ابوحنیفہ ایک جانب ہوں اور دوسری جانب ان کے اصحاب یا صاحبین ہوں تو اگر ان کے اختلاف کا دارو مدار عصر و زمان پر ہو، بایں طور کہ امام صاحب نے اپنے زمانہ کے احوال اور عرف کے مطابق فتوی دیا ہو اور اصحاب کے زمانہ میں عرف اور احوال میں تبدیلی ہونے کی وجہ سے اصحاب نے دوسرے قول پر فتوی دیا ہو تو اس صورت میں صاحبین اور دیگر اصحاب کا قول ہی راجح ہوگا، کیوں کہ عرف کی تبدیلی سے احکام میں تبدیلی ہوتی رہتی ہے۔ (شرح عقود رسم المفتی:۱۰۳،فتاوی قاضی خان: ۹/۱)
Sixth Principle: The disagreement of Imam A'zam and the two companions is based on time and era.
When in a matter Imam Abu Hanifa is on one side and his companions or the two companions on the other side, then if their disagreement depends on time and era, in the sense that Imam Sahib gave fatwa according to the circumstances and custom of his time, and due to change in custom and circumstances in the time of the companions, they gave fatwa on the other saying, then in this case the saying of the two companions and other companions will be preponderant, because rulings change with the change of custom.
(Sharh Uqud Rasm al-Mufti: 103, Fatawa Qadhi Khan: 9/1)
Source pages p. 53

Commentary
This sixth principle introduces an important consideration: the role of changing customs and circumstances (taghayyur al-urf wa al-ahwal). When Imam Abu Hanifa differed from his students (Abu Yusuf, Muhammad, and others) on a matter that is clearly tied to prevailing customs, and those customs changed in the students' era, then the students' opinion becomes preponderant. The reasoning is that rulings can change based on custom ('adah muhakkamah). This principle reflects the Hanafi school's sensitivity to context and its recognition that some rulings are time-bound. The mufti in later times would follow the opinion that matches contemporary custom, which is often the later opinion.

Key Terms
عرف (Urf): Custom or prevailing practice that can influence legal rulings, especially when it changes over time.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

B) The Sahibayn's opinion is supported by a stronger hadith.
C) The disagreement is based on a change in custom and circumstances over time.
D) Later scholars always prefer the opinion of students over the teacher.

Key Takeaway
When Imam Abu Hanifa and his companions differ due to changing custom, the companions' later opinion becomes the operative position in fatwa.

When Imam Abu Hanifa and the Sahibayn Differ Without Custom – Whose View to Follow?
Rasm al-Mufti rule on mufti hierarchy when custom is not a factor.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the two scholarly positions regarding which opinion (Imam Abu Hanifa or the Sahibayn) to follow when the difference is not based on custom, and state the implications for a mufti's discretion.

Core Idea
When Imam and Sahibayn differ without time/custom basis, later scholars disagree: must the mufti always follow Imam or has discretion?

This lesson addresses a nuanced scenario in Hanafi fatwa methodology: when the difference between Imam Abu Hanifa and his two companions (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad) does not stem from changing customs or time periods, which opinion should the mufti adopt? The text presents two views among later Hanafi scholars themselves, showing that even within the school, matters of hierarchy are sometimes contested. Understanding this principle helps a mufti navigate cases where no explicit tarjih exists.

Guided Path
1
Recognize the scenario
Identify that the disagreement between Imam Abu Hanifa and the Sahibayn is not based on changing customs or time. This is the key condition for applying this principle.
If the disagreement were based on custom, a different rule would apply.
2
Identify the first view
The first view, attributed to Abdullah ibn Mubarak, holds that the mufti must always give fatwa according to Imam Abu Hanifa's opinion and cannot depart from it.
This view emphasizes strict adherence to the founder of the school.
3
Identify the second view
The second view, which is incomplete in the original text, gives the mufti absolute choice: he may follow either Imam Abu Hanifa or the Sahibayn as he wishes.
The passage cuts off, but typical completions affirm full discretion.
4
Understand the open-endedness
Note that the text does not provide a clear preference (tarjih) between these two views. This shows that even fundamental procedural rules can be subject to ikhtilaf among scholars.
A mufti must be aware of this ikhtilaf and may need to consult other sources or follow the majority practice.
5
Apply to fatwa practice
In practice, when a mufti encounters a case where Imam Abu Hanifa and the Sahibayn differ and the difference is not due to custom, he should recognize that his choice may be guided by one of these two views. He may adopt the more cautious approach (always following Imam) or exercise discretion based on his own ijtihad or the established practice of his teachers.
The mufti should document his reasoning and be consistent.

Source Text

جب کسی مسئلہ میں امام ابو حنیفہ ایک جانب ہوں اور دوسری جانب ان کے اصحاب یا صاحبین ہوں اور ان کے اختلاف کا دارو مدار عصر و زمان پر نہ ہو تو کن کے قول پر فتوی دیں گے اس سلسلہ میں علماء کے درمیان اختلاف ہے: پہلا قول یہ ہے کہ بہر صورت امام اعظم کے قول پر فتوی دیں گے اور اس سے عدول نہیں کریں گے، یہ حضرت عبد اللہ بن مبارک کا قول ہے، دوسرا قول یہ ہے کہ مفتی کو مطلقا اختیار ہوگا ، اگر چاہے تو امام اعظم کے قول پر فتوی
